Ibn Taymiyya's First Proof: Definition as Mere Claim Does Not Inform
Ibn Taymiyya presents the first proof that definitions do not convey knowledge of the defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's first proof against the epistemological value of definitions and evaluate its strength.

Core Idea
Definitions are mere claims; without proof, they cannot produce certain knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the standard logician's claim that definitions (hudud) convey the essence of the defined. His first proof is a powerful epistemological critique: a definition is just a declarative statement from the definer, and the listener either already knows it or must accept it on blind trust. In this lesson, you will walk through his argument step by step and see why even the logicians' refined notions of definitions as single terms fail to escape his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
Ibn Taymiyya is targeting the claim that definitions (hudud) provide knowledge (taswir) of the defined (mahdud). Find where he states this target in the Arabic.
See line 2: 'لا يفيد معرفة المحدود'.
2
Analyse his dilemma
He splits the listener into two cases: either the listener already knows the meaning or does not. For each case, explain why the definition fails to convey new knowledge.
This is a classic disjunctive argument (istithna'i).
3
Consider the logicians' objection
Some logicians reply that definitions are not full sentences but single terms. Identify this objection in the text and how Ibn Taymiyya refutes it.
He says even single terms are mere labels without proof.
4
Evaluate the critique's strength
Does Ibn Taymiyya's argument convincingly defeat the logician's theory of definition? Consider whether definitions might still have a pedagogical or clarifying role.
This encourages critical engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: الحد - سواءٌ جُعل مركباً أو مفرداً - لا يفيد معرفة المحدود. أحدها: إن الحدّ مجرّد قول الحادّ ودعواه، فإنه إذا قال: حدّ «الإنسان» مثلاً: «إنه الحيوان الناطق أو الضاحك»، فهذه قضيّة خبرية، ومجرّد دعوى خليّة عن حجّة. فإما أن يكون المستمع لها عالماً يصدقها بدون هذا القول وإما أن لا يكون. فإن كان عالماً بذلك ثبت أنه لم يستفد هذه المعرفة بهذا الحد، وإن لم يكن عالماً، يصدّقه بمجرد قول المخبر الذي لا دليل معه لا يفيده العلم كيف وهو يعلم أنه ليس بمعصوم في قوله؟ فقد تبين أنه على التقديرين ليس الحدّ هو الذي يفيده معرفة المحدود. فإن قيل: الحدّ ليس هو الجملة الخبرية، وإنما هو مجرّد قولك حيوان ناطق، غير مفيد وهذا مفرد لا جملة - وهذا السؤال وارد على أحد اصطلاحيهم؛ فإنهم تارة يجعلون الحد هو الجملة التامة كما يقوله الغزالي وغيره، وتارة يجعلون الحد هو المفرد المقيَّد كالاسم وهو الذي يسمونه «التركيب» التقييدي، كما يذكر ذلك الرازي ونحوه - قيل: التكلم بالمفرد لا يفيد ولا يكون جواباً لسائل، سواء كان موصوفا مركباً تركيباً تقييدياً أو لم يكن كذلك.
The first proof: The definition - whether taken as compound or single - does not convey knowledge of the defined. One of them: The definition is merely the statement of the definer and his claim. For if he says, defining 'human' for example: 'It is the rational or laughing animal,' this is a declarative statement and a mere claim devoid of proof. So either the listener is already knowledgeable and accepts it without this statement, or he is not. If he was knowledgeable of it, then it is established that he did not gain this knowledge from this definition. And if he was not knowledgeable, then he trusts it merely by the statement of the informer who has no proof with him; this does not give him knowledge, especially since he knows that he is not infallible in his statement. So it has become clear that in both cases, the definition is not what gives him knowledge of the defined. If it is said: The definition is not the declarative sentence, but rather it is merely your saying 'rational animal,' which is not informative and is a single expression not a sentence - and this question comes from one of their terminologies; for sometimes they make the definition the complete sentence as al-Ghazali and others say, and sometimes they make the definition the restricted single expression like the name, which they call 'restrictive composition,' as al-Razi and others mention - it is said: Speaking with a single expression does not inform and is not an answer to a questioner, whether it is described as compound by restrictive composition or not.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians' claim that definitions (حدود) convey knowledge of the essences of things. He presents the first proof. The argument is structured as follows: A definition is either known to the listener or not. If the listener already knows the meaning, the definition adds nothing. If he does not know, then he must trust the definer's mere statement without any evidence, which cannot produce certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya supports this with the fact that the definer is not infallible. He then anticipates an objection from the logicians who distinguish between definitions as complete sentences (like 'human is a rational animal') and definitions as single expressions (like 'rational animal'). Some logicians, like al-Ghazali, define the definition as a complete sentence, while others, like al-Razi, define it as a single restrictive expression. Ibn Taymiyya responds that even a single expression cannot serve as an answer to a question, because it merely labels without providing evidence. Thus, whether the definition is a sentence or a single term, it fails to convey new knowledge of the defined.

Key Terms
الحد
المحدود
قول
دعوى
جملة خبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It conveys the essence if the definition is a single term.
D) It is only useful for those who already know.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's first proof shows that definitions, as mere declarative claims, cannot convey new knowledge without accompanying evidence.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Single-Term Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues definition as single term functions like a name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a definition treated as a single term only indicates the name, not the reality.

Core Idea
A single-term definition tells you the name, but not whether it matches reality.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of Aristotelian definitions by focusing on the claim that a definition, even when expressed as a single term, reveals the essence. He argues that if a definition is a single term, it functions merely as a name, indicating the concept but not verifying its correspondence to reality. This lesson walks through his linguistic and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's target
Find where he states that if the definition is a single expression (مفرد), then its indication is like that of a name. This is the core claim being challenged.
2
Analyze the linguistic examples
Notice the Arabic examples: 'Food' as an answer implies 'This is food.' Quranic verses like 'They will say: Allah' function similarly. These show that single words in context act as complete sentences, but they only predicate something without proving identity.
Ibn Taymiyya uses these to show that a single term can only give conception, not establish truth beyond that.
3
Understand the epistemological limit
He argues that even if the questioner conceives of the referent (مسمى), he does not know whether that referent is the actual reality (حقيقة) of the defined. The definition does not provide that proof.
This is the key critique: definition as a single term gives only tasawwur (conception), not tasdiq (assent) about correspondence.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that such a definition's indication is merely like that of a name. He aligns with 'the people of correctness' who say the definition is a detailed explanation (تفصيل) of what the name indicates.
This implies that a proper definition must be composite, specifying genus and differentia, to go beyond a mere name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم. ولهذا لما مَرَّ بعضُ العرب بمؤذن يقول: «أشهد أن محمداً رسول الله» - بالنصب - قال: «فَعَلَ ماذا؟» فإذا قيل: ما هذا؟ قيل: «طعام»، فهذا خبر مبتدإ محذوف باتفاق الناس، تقديره «هذا طعام»، كقوله تعالى: {وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ اللَّهُ} [الزمر: 38]، وقوله: {قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ الَّذِي جَاءَ بِهِ مُوسَىٰ نُورًا وَهُدًى لِّلنَّاسِ ... قُلِ اللَّهُ} [الأنعام: 91]، وكقوله: {سَيَقُولُونَ ثَلَاثَةٌ رَّابِعُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ وَيَقُولُونَ خَمْسَةٌ سَادِسُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ رَجْمًا بِالْغَيْبِ وَيَقُولُونَ سَبْعَةٌ وَثَامِنُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ} [الكهف: 22] أي «هم ثلاثة» و«هم خمسة» و«هم سبعة». ومنه قوله تعالى: {وَقُلِ الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ} [الكهف: 29]، أي «هذا الحق من ربكم»، ليس كما يظنه بعض الجهال، أي «قل القول الحق»، فإنّ هذا لو أريد لنصب لفظ «الحق». والمراد إثبات أن القرآن حق، ولهذا قال: {الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ}، ليس المراد هنا بقول حق مطلق؛ بل هذا المعنى مذكور في قوله: {وَإِذَا قُلْتُمْ فَاعْدِلُوا} [الأنعام: 152]، وقوله: {أَلَمْ يُؤْخَذْ عَلَيْهِم مِّيثَاقُ الْكِتَابِ أَن لَّا يَقُولُوا عَلَى اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْحَقَّ} [الأعراف: 169]. ثم إذا قُدّر أن الحد هو المفرد فالمفردات أسماء، وغاية السائل أن يتصوَّر مسماها، لكن مَن أين له إذا تصور مُسَمَّاها أن هذا المسمَّى هو المسئول عنه، وأن هذا المسمى هو حقيقة المحدود؟ فإن قيل: يفيده مجرد تصوّر المسمى من غير أن يُحكم أنه هو ذلك أو غير ذلك، بل دلالة الحد تصوّر «إنسان»، قيل: فحينئذ يكون هذا كمجرّد دِلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه، الاسم على مسماه، كما لو قيل: «الإنسان». وهذا يحقق ما قلناه من أن دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، وهذا هو قول أهل الصواب الذين يقولون: «الحدّ تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم».
The second position: The indication of the definition is like the indication of the name. That is why when some Arab passed by a caller to prayer saying 'I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of God' with the accusative, he said: 'He did what?' When it is said: 'What is this?' it is said: 'Food.' This is a predicate of an omitted subject by consensus, its implied form being 'This is food.' Like His saying: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah.' And His saying: 'Say: Who sent down the Book that Moses came with as a light and guidance for the people? ... Say: Allah.' And like His saying: 'They will say: Three, their dog being the fourth; and they say: Five, their dog being the sixth, guessing in the unseen; and they say: Seven, and their dog being the eighth.' Meaning 'They are three,' 'They are five,' and 'They are seven.' And from this His saying: 'And say: The truth is from your Lord.' Meaning 'This truth is from your Lord,' not as some ignorant people think—i.e., 'Say the truth statement,' because if that were intended, the word 'truth' would be accusative. The purpose is to establish that the Qur'an is truth; that is why He said 'The truth is from your Lord.' The meaning here is not just any truth statement; rather this meaning is mentioned in His saying: 'And when you speak, be just' and His saying: 'Was the covenant of the Book not taken upon them that they should not say about Allah except the truth?' Then if it is assumed that the definition is the single expression, then single expressions are names, and the utmost the questioner can do is to conceive of its referent. But how does he know, when he conceives of its referent, that this referent is what was asked about, and that this referent is the reality of the defined? If it is said: It gives him merely the conception of the referent without judging that it is that or something else—rather the indication of the definition is the conception of 'human'—it is said: In that case, this is like the mere indication of a single expression to its meaning, the name to its referent, as if one said: 'human.' And this confirms what we said, that the indication of the definition is like the indication of the name. And this is the statement of the people of correctness who say: 'The definition is a detailed explanation of what the name indicates.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definitions, now focusing on the case where the definition is considered a single expression (مفرد). He argues that such a definition functions no differently from a name: it merely indicates a concept without verifying that the concept corresponds to the reality of the defined. To support his claim, he provides linguistic examples from Arabic grammar, showing that single words often imply a complete sentence when taken in context. For instance, the word 'food' as an answer is understood as a complete sentence 'This is food.' Similarly, Quranic verses like {وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم ... لَيَقُولُنَّ اللَّهُ} show that the word 'Allah' is a predicate for an omitted subject. Ibn Taymiyya then returns to the definition: if the definition is a single term, it gives only the conception of the thing (like 'human'), but it does not establish that this conception is the true essence of the defined. The questioner might know what 'human' means, but he does not know if that meaning is the reality (حقيقة) of what was asked about. Thus, the definition's indication is merely that of a name—it labels but does not prove. He concludes by aligning himself with 'the people of correctness' who say that the definition is a detailed explanation of what the name indicates, implying that the definition adds no new epistemological content beyond the name itself.

Key Terms

الاسم
دلالة
مسمَّى
حقيقة

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, when a definition is taken as a single expression (مفرد), what does it actually provide?
A) A proof that the concept matches reality.
B) A complete sentence that informs the listener.
C) Merely the conception of the referent, like a name.
D) A detailed analysis of the essence.

Key Takeaway
A definition as a single term functions like a name: it indicates a concept but does not verify that the concept corresponds to reality.

Meaningful Speech and the Logicians' Contradiction
Ibn Taymiyya states that only complete sentences convey meaning, rejecting single words as speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's definition of meaningful speech and explain his critique of the logicians' classification of pronouns as particles.

Core Idea
Meaningful speech is only a complete sentence; logicians contradict their own classification.

Ibn Taymiyya begins with a universal agreement: meaningful speech must be a complete sentence. He notes that logicians initially agree but then contradict themselves by calling pronouns (which grammarians consider nouns) particles. This lesson unpacks his argument and equips you to detect such classificatory inconsistencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal agreement
Read the first sentence: Ibn Taymiyya states that all people of the earth agree that meaningful speech (al-kalām al-mufīd) is only a complete sentence, like two nouns or a verb and a noun. This is the baseline definition.
2
Find the logicians' initial agreement
Ibn Taymiyya says the logicians acknowledged this division of words into noun, word (verb), and particle. They claim to follow the same principle.
Note: By 'word' they mean verb, matching grammarians.
3
Pinpoint the contradiction
The logicians classify pronouns—like 'him' in 'I saw him'—as particles. But grammarians call them 'implicit nouns'. This contradicts their own classification, since pronouns are nouns, not particles.
Pronouns are considered nouns in Arabic grammar.
4
Understand the consequence
This inconsistency undermines the logicians' entire classification. It shows they do not consistently apply their own rules, making their framework unreliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام المفيد ولهذا كان من المتفق عليه بين جميع أهل الأرض أن الكلام المفيد لا يكون إلا جملة تامة جملة تامة، كاسمين، أو فعل واسم. وهذا مما اعترف به المنطقيون، وقسموا الألفاظ إلى «اسم» و«كلمة» و«حرف» - يسمّى «أداة» ؛ وقالوا : المراد بـ «الكلمة» ما يريده النحاة بلفظ «الفعل». لكنهم مع هذا يتناقضون ويجعلون ما هو «اسم» عند النحاة (حر) فا» في اصطلاحهم. كالضمائر - ضمائر الرفع والنصب والجر المتصلة، والمنفصلة - مثل قولك: «رأيتُه» و«مَرَّ بي»، فإن هذه أسماء، ويسمّيها النحاة (الأسماء المضمرة؛ والمنطقيون يقولون : إنها لغة اليونان من باب الحروف»، ويسمّونها الخوالف»، كأنها خَلَفٌ الأسماء عن الظاهرة . فأما الاسم المفرد فلا يكون كلاماً مفيداً عند أحدٍ من أهل الأرض، بل ولا أهل السماء. وإن كان وحده كان معه غيره مضمراً، أو كان المقصود به تنبيهاً وإشارة كما يقصد بالأصوات التي لم توضع لمعنى ؛ لا أنه تقصد به المعاني التي تقصد بالكلام. استطراد (۱): ولهذا عدَّ الناسُ من البدع ما يفعله النساك من ذكر اسم الله وحده، بدون تأليف كلام. فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : أفضلُ الذكر لا إله إلا الله»، وأفضل الدعاء «الحمد لله والله أكبر».
Meaningful speech: and for this reason it is agreed upon among all people of the earth that meaningful speech is only a complete sentence, like two nouns or a verb and a noun. And this is what the logicians acknowledged, and they divided words into 'noun', 'word', and 'particle' - called 'tool'; and they said: by 'word' they mean what grammarians mean by 'verb'. But despite this they contradict themselves and make what is a 'noun' for grammarians a 'particle' in their terminology. Like pronouns - pronouns of nominative, accusative, and genitive, attached and detached - such as your saying 'I saw him' and 'he passed by me', for these are nouns, and grammarians call them 'implicit nouns'; and the logicians say: in the Greek language they are from the category of 'particles', and they call them 'substitutes', as if they are substitutes for explicit nouns. As for the single noun, it is not meaningful speech according to anyone on earth, nor even the inhabitants of heaven. And if it is alone, there is with it something implied, or it is intended as a warning and indication, as in sounds not assigned to meaning; it is not intended for the meanings intended by speech. Digression (1): For this reason people consider as innovation what the ascetics do of mentioning the name of Allah alone, without composing speech. For the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The best remembrance is 'There is no god but Allah', and the best supplication is 'Praise be to Allah' and 'Allah is Greatest'.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' classification of words and a declaration that meaningful speech (al-kalām al-mufīd) must be a complete sentence, consisting of at least two nouns or a verb and a noun. Ibn Taymiyya notes that logicians initially agree with this but then contradict themselves by classifying pronouns (which grammarians consider nouns) as particles (or tools). He gives examples: 'I saw him' and 'he passed by me' - these pronouns are nouns in Arabic grammar, but logicians call them 'substitutes' or particles in their own terminology. He then asserts that a single noun cannot be meaningful speech according to any rational person or even heavenly beings. If a single word is uttered, it must have an implied element or be meant as a mere signal, not as proper speech conveying intended meanings. He then digresses to apply this to religious practice: saying 'Allah' alone (without a predicate) is considered an innovation (bid'a), as the Prophet taught complete sentences for dhikr like 'La ilaha illa Allah' and 'Alhamdulillah wa Allahu Akbar'.

Key Terms
كلام مفيد
اسم
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the logicians call pronouns according to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage?
A) Nouns
B) Verbs
C) Particles
D) Sentences

Key Takeaway
Meaningful speech must be a complete sentence; the logicians' misclassification of pronouns reveals an inconsistency in their own system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Linguistic Innovation of Saying 'Allah' Alone
Ibn Taymiyya states that only complete sentences convey meaning, rejecting single words as speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic criterion to evaluate religious practices and explain why saying a single word as dhikr is considered an innovation.

Core Idea
Saying 'Allah' alone is not meaningful speech; it is an innovation because the Prophet taught complete sentences.

Ibn Taymiyya extends his linguistic principle to religious practice, criticizing the practice of repeating the name 'Allah' alone without forming a complete sentence. He argues that this is an innovation (bid'a) because it contradicts the Prophet's teaching of complete dhikr sentences like 'La ilaha illa Allah'. This lesson examines his critique and its basis in the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of meaningful speech
From the previous lesson, you know that Ibn Taymiyya defines meaningful speech as a complete sentence (two nouns or verb+noun). A single noun cannot convey a meaningful message on its own.
Refer back to the first part of the passage.
2
Apply the definition to the dhikr practice
The practice of saying 'Allah' alone is a single noun. It does not form a complete sentence. According to Ibn Taymiyya, it is therefore not meaningful speech.
3
Contrast with prophetic dhikr
The Prophet (peace be upon him) taught dhikr in complete sentences: 'La ilaha illa Allah' (there is no god but Allah), 'Alhamdulillah' (praise be to Allah), 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is Greatest). These are all complete sentences.
Note: 'Allahu Akbar' is a complete sentence (noun + predicate).
4
Conclude why it is an innovation
Since the practice of saying 'Allah' alone deviates from the prophetic model and violates the linguistic principle of meaningful speech, it is classified as a religious innovation (bid'a).

And this is what the logicians acknowledged, and they divided words into 'noun', 'word', and 'particle' - called 'tool'; and they said: by 'word' they mean what grammarians mean by 'verb'. But despite this they contradict themselves and make what is a 'noun' for grammarians a 'particle' in their terminology. Like pronouns - pronouns of nominative, accusative, and genitive, attached and detached - such as your saying 'I saw him' and 'he passed by me', for these are nouns, and grammarians call them 'implicit nouns'; and the logicians say: in the Greek language they are from the category of 'particles', and they call them 'substitutes', as if they are substitutes for explicit nouns. For the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The best remembrance is 'There is no god but Allah', and the best supplication is 'Praise be to Allah' and 'Allah is Greatest'.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definitions as Solitary Reports
Ibn Taymiyya critiques definitions as unproven claims like khabar wahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definitions as solitary reports and evaluate its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Definitions, like solitary reports without evidence, cannot yield certain knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide certain knowledge of the defined. He argues that definitions are mere solitary reports (khabar wahid) without any proof, and thus cannot produce epistemic certainty. This lesson unpacks his argument and its significance for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Read the passage and find where Ibn Taymiyya states that logicians claim definitions provide conception (tasawwur) as a type of knowledge.
Look for the terms 'had' and 'tasawwur'.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection to 'bare conception'
He argues that conception without any affirmation or negation is not knowledge but mere fancy, using examples like 'sea of quicksilver'.
This establishes that definitions fail to produce true knowledge.
3
Analyze the analogy to solitary reports
Ibn Taymiyya compares definitions to khabar wahid: both are claims without proof, so they cannot be relied upon for certainty.
He highlights the inconsistency of logicians who criticize solitary reports but use definitions.
4
Evaluate the condition for definitions to be useful
He concedes that if the listener already knows the defined, the definition can label it, but then it does not provide new knowledge.
This shows definitions are epistemically dependent on prior knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الثاني : خبر الواحد بلا دليل لا يفيد العلم

ثم قد بينا فساد قولهم سواءً جُعل الحدّ مفرداً كالأسماء، أو مركباً كالجمل ؛ وأنه على التقديرين لا يفيد تصوير المسمى . وهو المطلوب.

و دعوى المدعي أن الحد مجرّد المفردِ المقيَّدِ كدعواهم أن التصوّر - الذي هو أحد نوعى العلم - هو التصوّر المجرَّد عن كل نفي وإثبات . ومعلوم أن مثل هذا لا يكون علماً عند أحد من العقلاء، بل إذا خطر ببال الإنسان شيء ما ولم يخطر له ثبوته ولا انتفاؤه بوجه من الوجوه لم يكن قد علم شيئاً ؛ مثل من خطر له «بحرُ زِنْبَقِ» أو «جبل ياقوت» خاطراً مجرّداً عن كون هذا التصور ثابتاً في الخارج أو منتفياً، ممكناً أو ممتنعاً، فإن هذا من جنس الوَسْواس، لا من جنس العلم.

وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا في مواضع متعددة، مثل الكلام على «المحصل»، وبينا أن المشروط في التصديق من جنس العلم المشروط في «القول». فمن صدق بما لم يتصوّره كان قد تكلم بغير علم، ومن صدق بما تصوّره كان كالمتكلم بعلم .. فقولهم في «الحدود القولية من جنس قولهم في «التصوّرات الذهنية».

[هامش: كون التصور ليس بعلم والمناطقة يجعلونه علماً، كما قال السعد: «العلم إن كان إذعاناً لنسبة فتصديق، وإلا فتصور»، فجعله قسماً من العلم والحق ما قاله المصنف رحمه الله .]

الوجه الثاني: خبر الواحد بلا دليل لا يفيد العلم

الثاني : أنهم يقولون الحدّ لا يمنع ولا يقوم عليه دليل، وإنما يمكن إبطاله بـ ( النقض) و(المعارضة)، ومنهم من يقول: إنما يمكن إبطاله بالنقض فقط دون المعارضة بخلاف القياس)، فإنه يمكن فيه (الممانعة) و(المعارضة)) .

فيقال : إذا لم يكن الحاد قد أقام دليلاً على صحة الحد امتنع أن يعرف المستمع المحدودَ به، إذا جَوّز عليه الخطأ، فإنه إذا لم يعرف صحة الحدّ إلا بقوله، وقوله محتمل للصدق والكذب، امتنع أن يعرفه بقوله

و من العجب أن هؤلاء يزعمون أن هذه طُرُقٌ عقلية يقينية، ويجعلون العلم بالمفرد أصلاً للعلم بالمركب ويجعلون العمدة في ذلك على الحد، الذي هو قول الحادّ بلا دليل، وهو خبر واحد عن أمر عقليّ لا حسي يحتمل الصواب والخطأ والصدق والكذب، ثم يعيبون على مَنْ يعتمد في الأمور السمعيَّة على نقل الواحد الذي القرائن ما يفيد المستمع العالم بها العلم اليقينيَّ، زاعمين أن خبر الواحد لا يفيد العلم. ولا ريب أن مجرد خبر الواحد الذي لا دليل على صدقه لا يفيد العلم، لكن هذا بعينه قولهم في الحد، فإنه خبر واحدٍ لا دليل على صدقه، بل ولا يمكن عندهم إقامة دليل على صدقه ; فلم يكن الحدّ مفيداً لتصور المحدود.

ولكن إن كان المستمع قد تَصَوَّر المحدود قبل هذا، أو تصوّره معه أو بعده بدون الحد، وعلم أن ذلك حدّه ، علم صدقه في حده، وحينئذ فلا يكون الحد أفاد التصوير .

وهذا بين

وتلخيصه أن تصور المحدود بالحدّ لا يمكن بدون العلم بصدق قول الحاد، وصدقُ قوله لا يعلم بمجرد الخبر، فلا يعلم المحدود بالحدّ.

ومن قال «أنا أريد بالحدّ (المفرد)» لم يكن قوله مفيداً لشيء أصلاً، فإن المتكلم بـ (الاسم المفرد لا يفيد المستمع شيئاً، بل إن كان تصوّر المحدود بدون هذا اللفظ كان قد تصوّره بدون هذا اللفظ المفرد، وإن لم يكن تصوّره بدون اللفظ المفرد لم يكن تصوره لا قبله ولا تصوّره به
Second Maqam - Second Wajh: The solitary report without evidence does not yield knowledge.

Then we have already demonstrated the corruption of their statement, whether the definition is made as a single word like nouns, or as a compound like sentences; and that in both cases it does not provide a depiction of the defined thing. That is the desired conclusion.

And the claimant's claim that the definition is merely the restricted single word, like their claim that conception – which is one of the two types of knowledge – is the conception stripped of all negation and affirmation. And it is known that such a thing is not knowledge according to any of the rational people. Rather, if something occurs to a person's mind and not its affirmation nor its negation occurs to him in any way, he has not known anything; like one to whom occurs 'a sea of quicksilver' or 'a mountain of ruby' as a mere idea, stripped of whether this conception is existing in the external world or not, possible or impossible – this is of the type of whispering (waswasa), not of the type of knowledge.

And we have elaborated on this in multiple places, such as the discussion on al-Muhassal, and we showed that what is required in assent is of the type of knowledge required in 'speech'. So whoever affirms something he has not conceived has spoken without knowledge, and whoever affirms something he has conceived is like one speaking with knowledge. So their statement about 'verbal definitions' is of the type of their statement about 'mental conceptions'.

[Margin: The conception is not knowledge, but the logicians make it knowledge, as al-Sa'd said: 'Knowledge if it is an assent to a proposition then it is tasdiq, otherwise it is tasawwur', so he made it a division of knowledge. The truth is what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) said.]

Second Wajh: The solitary report without evidence does not yield knowledge.

Second: They say that the definition does not prevent [error] and no proof can be established for it, and it can only be refuted by counterexample (naqd) and opposition (mu'arada). Some of them say it can only be refuted by counterexample without opposition, unlike the syllogism, because in it disproof (mumana'a) and opposition are possible.

So it is said: If the definer has not provided evidence for the correctness of the definition, it is impossible for the listener to know the defined thing by it, if error is considered possible regarding him. For if he does not know the correctness of the definition except by his statement, and his statement is subject to truth and falsehood, it is impossible for him to know it by his statement.

And it is astonishing that these people claim that these [definitions] are certain rational methods, and they make knowledge of the single term the foundation for knowledge of the compound, and they rely on the definition, which is the statement of the definer without evidence, and it is a single report about an intellectual matter, not a sensory one, subject to correctness and error, truth and falsehood. Then they fault those who rely in auditory matters on a single transmission accompanied by contextual indicators that give the listener who is aware of them certain knowledge, claiming that a solitary report does not yield knowledge. There is no doubt that a mere solitary report without evidence for its truthfulness does not yield knowledge, but this is exactly their statement about the definition: it is a single report with no evidence for its truthfulness, and moreover, according to them, it is impossible to establish evidence for its truthfulness. So the definition does not provide a conception of the defined.

However, if the listener had conceived the defined before this, or he conceives it along with or after the definition without the definition, and knows that this is its definition, then he knows its truthfulness in his definition, and in that case the definition did not provide the conception.

This is clear.

The summary: Conception of the defined by means of the definition is impossible without knowledge of the truthfulness of the definer's statement, and the truthfulness of his statement is not known by mere report. So the defined is not known by the definition.

And whoever says 'I mean by definition the single word' – his statement is not useful at all, for the speaker of a single word does not convey anything to the listener. Rather, if the listener had conceived the defined without this word, he would have conceived it without this single word; and if he had not conceived it without the single word, he would not have conceived it before nor does he conceive it by it.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide knowledge (ilm) of the defined. It is part of his second argument (al-wajh al-thani) in the second station (al-maqam al-thani). He begins by reiterating his earlier demonstration that definitions, whether expressed as single terms (mufrad) like nouns or as compounds (murakkab) like sentences, fail to produce a depiction (taswir) of the defined. He then attacks the logicians' notion of conception (tasawwur) as a type of knowledge stripped of any affirmation (ithbat) or negation (nafy), arguing that such a bare notion is not knowledge at all but mere fancy (waswasa). He provides examples like 'a sea of quicksilver' or 'a mountain of ruby' to illustrate that a conception without any judgment about existence or possibility does not constitute knowledge. He references his earlier works where he expanded on this point, particularly the relationship between conception and assent (tasdiq). A marginal note confirms that according to the author, conception alone is not knowledge, contrary to the logicians who treat it as a division of knowledge.

Then he moves to the core of the second argument: definitions are like solitary reports (khabar wahid) without evidence. He notes that the logicians themselves admit that definitions can only be refuted by counterexample (naqd) and opposition (mu'arada), not proven. Thus, the definer's statement is a claim with no supporting proof. If the listener considers the possibility of error, he cannot gain knowledge from the definer's mere assertion. Ibn Taymiyya highlights the inconsistency of the logicians: they claim their definitions are certain rational methods, yet they criticize the use of solitary reports in religious (sam'iyya) matters, even when those reports are accompanied by contextual indicators (qara'in) that yield certainty. In fact, definitions are themselves solitary reports about intellectual matters without any evidence. The only way a definition could be useful is if the listener already knows the defined thing independently, in which case the definition does not provide new knowledge but merely labels something already known. The summary is that knowledge of the defined through definition requires prior knowledge of the definer's truthfulness, which cannot be known from the report alone. Finally, he dismisses the notion that a definition is just a single word (mufrad), arguing that a single word conveys nothing unless the listener already knows the concept; otherwise it is useless.

Key Terms
حد
تصور
تصديق
خبر واحد
علم

Key Takeaway
Definitions cannot produce knowledge without evidence; they are epistemically analogous to solitary reports and require prior knowledge of the definer's truthfulness.

Why Definition Cannot Produce Certainty: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that definition produces knowledge, comparing it to a single reporter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a definition (hadd) is epistemically equivalent to a khabar wahid and identify the flaw in the logicians' claim that definition yields conceptual knowledge.

Core Idea
Definition is just a single reporter – no better than a khabar wahid without proof.

Ibn Taymiyya targets the foundational claim of Aristotelian logic that definition (hadd) produces certain conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). He draws a striking analogy: the logicians' definition is nothing but a single person's report about an intellectual matter, susceptible to error just like a khabar wahid in Islamic jurisprudence. In this lesson, we will unpack his analogy and see how it undermines the certainty logicians attribute to definition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the analogy
Find where Ibn Taymiyya explicitly compares hadd to khabar wahid. He says: 'وهو خبر واحد عن أمر عقليّ' – a single report about an intellectual matter.
This is the core analogical claim.
2
Identify the premises of the analogical argument
The logicians claim definition yields certain knowledge (tasawwur). But a khabar wahid without proof does not yield certainty. Since hadd is a khabar wahid, it too cannot yield certainty.
The analogy rests on the nature of the report, not its content.
3
Examine the counter-argument and response
Ibn Taymiyya considers that maybe the listener already knows the defined. If so, the definition is redundant. If not, the definition is useless. Summarize this dilemma.
This dilemma shows that definition cannot be the source of new conceptual knowledge.
4
Refute the 'singular term' dodge
Some might say definition is just the singular term. But a singular term conveys no information on its own. Show that this does not produce conceptualization.
Ibn Taymiyya closes off a potential escape for the logicians.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Definition cannot be the source of conceptual knowledge; any knowledge must come from elsewhere.
This is the main takeaway for the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أن هؤلاء يزعمون أن هذه طُرُقٌ عقلية يقينية، ويجعلون العلم بالمفرد أصلاً للعلم بالمركب ويجعلون العمدة في ذلك على الحد، الذي هو قول الحادّ بلا دليل، وهو خبر واحد عن أمر عقليّ لا حسي يحتمل الصواب والخطأ والصدق والكذب، ثم يعيبون على مَنْ يعتمد في الأمور السمعيَّة على نقل الواحد الذي القرائن ما يفيد المستمع العالم بها العلم اليقينيَّ، زاعمين أن خبر الواحد لا يفيد العلم. ولا ريب أن مجرد خبر الواحد الذي لا دليل على صدقه لا يفيد العلم، لكن هذا بعينه قولهم في الحد، فإنه خبر واحدٍ لا دليل على صدقه، بل ولا يمكن عندهم إقامة دليل على صدقه ؛ فلم يكن الحدّ مفيداً لتصور المحدود. ولكن إن كان المستمع قد تَصَوَّر المحدود قبل هذا، أو تصوَّره معه أو بعده بدون الحد، وعلم أن ذلك حدّه ، علم صدقه في حده، وحينئذ فلا يكون الحد أفاد التصوير . وهذا بين. وتلخيصه أن تصور المحدود بالحدّ لا يمكن بدون العلم بصدق قول الحاد، وصدقُ قوله لا يعلم بمجرد الخبر، فلا يعلم المحدود بالحدّ. ومن قال «أنا أريد بالحدّ (المفرد)» لم يكن قوله مفيداً لشيء أصلاً، فإن المتكلم بـ (الاسم المفرد لا يفيد المستمع شيئاً، بل إن كان تصوّر المحدود بدون هذا اللفظ كان قد تصوّره بدون هذا اللفظ المفرد، وإن لم يكن تصوّره بدون اللفظ المفرد لم يكن تصوره لا قبله ولا تصوّره به.
It is astonishing that these people claim that these are certain rational methods, and they make knowledge of the simple term the foundation for knowledge of the compound, and they rely primarily on definition (hadd), which is the statement of the definer without proof, a single report about an intellectual matter, not sensory, susceptible to correctness and error, truth and falsehood. Then they criticize those who rely in auditory matters on a single transmission accompanied by indications that give the knowledgeable listener certain knowledge, claiming that a single report does not produce knowledge. Undoubtedly, a mere single report without proof of its truth does not produce knowledge, but this is exactly their own position regarding definition: it is a single report without proof of its truth, and according to them it is even impossible to establish proof of its truth. So definition is not useful for conceptualizing the defined. However, if the listener already conceptualized the defined before this, or conceptualized it simultaneously or after without the definition, and knew that that was its definition, then he knows the truth of the definition, and in that case the definition did not produce conceptualization. This is clear. The summary is that conceptualizing the defined by definition is impossible without knowledge of the truth of the definer's statement, and the truth of his statement is not known by mere report, so the defined is not known by definition. And whoever says 'I mean by definition the singular [word]'—his statement is not useful at all, because a speaker uttering a singular word does not inform the listener of anything. Rather, if the listener had conceptualized the defined without this word, he had already conceptualized it without the singular word, and if he had not conceptualized it without the singular word, then he does not conceptualize it either before or by it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This is a critical passage where Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that definition (hadd) is a reliable method for acquiring concepts (tasawwur). He draws an analogy between definition and a single-person report (khabar wahid) in legal/religious transmission. The logicians criticize reliance on solitary reports in Shar‘i matters, yet they themselves rely on definition—a single statement without evidence—to claim certain conceptual knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya argues that just as a khabar wahid without supporting evidence does not yield certainty, a definition without proof of its truth cannot produce conceptualization. He then addresses a possible counter-argument: if the listener already knows the defined, the definition is redundant; if not, the definition is useless because it is an unsupported claim. Finally, he refutes the suggestion that definition is merely the singular term, showing that a singular term conveys no information on its own. All examples here are rooted in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam discussions of khabar wahid.

Key Terms
الحد (al-ḥadd): A definition consisting of genus and differentia, claimed by logicians to produce conceptual knowledge; Ibn Taymiyya argues it is a single report with no proof. [Note: In standard Mantiq, hadd is a primary tool for definition; Ibn Taymiyya polemically redefines it as an unreliable report.]
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A report transmitted by a single person, which in Islamic legal theory may not yield certain knowledge without corroboration; Ibn Taymiyya compares it to hadd. [Note: In Usul al-Fiqh, khabar wahid is generally accepted for action but not for certainty; here used as a critical analogy.]
تصور (taṣawwur): Conceptualization or forming a mental image of a thing; Ibn Taymiyya argues it cannot be produced by a mere definition. [Note: In standard Mantiq, tasawwur is the goal of definition; Ibn Taymiyya limits it to prior or concurrent knowledge.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mutawatir report
B) Khabar wahid (single report)
C) Hadith qudsi
D) Qiyas al-'illa

Key Takeaway
Definition, like a single report (khabar wahid), cannot produce certain conceptual knowledge unless supported by independent evidence.

Ibn Taymiyya's Circularity Argument Against Definition
Ibn Taymiyya presents the third argument against definition: knowing the definition's correctness presupposes knowing the defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument against definition and identify the epistemological problem it raises.

Core Idea
Definition cannot produce new knowledge because verifying it requires prior knowledge of the defined.

In this lesson, we examine Ibn Taymiyya's third and most penetrating argument against the logical claim that definition (ḥadd) provides conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur). He reveals a vicious circularity: if definition is the path to knowing the defined, then we must already know the defined to verify the definition. This undermines the entire project of definition as a foundational tool in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Recall that logicians assert that ḥadd (definition) is the means to achieve a conceptualization (taṣawwur) of the defined (maḥdūd). Ibn Taymiyya attacks this claim.
The logicians treat definition as a 'revealer' (kāshif) of the concept.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya says: if definition produces conceptualization, then the conceptualization occurs after using the definition. But before using it, one must know that the definition is correct (ṣaḥīḥ), because a faulty definition would mislead.
This is the first step in exposing circularity.
3
Extract the circularity
Knowledge of the definition's correctness, however, depends on already knowing the defined—since the definition is a report (khabar) about the defined. So we would need to know the defined before the definition, yet the definition is supposed to give us that knowledge. This is impossible unless we accept the definition on authority (taqlīd).
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states: 'it is impossible to know the validity of the report before conceptualizing the reported-about'.
4
Understand the report analogy
Ibn Taymiyya treats definition as a type of report (khabar). For any report, verifying its truth requires prior knowledge of what it is about (the subject). Similarly, a definition cannot be verified without prior knowledge of the defined. This analogy is key to his refutation.
In standard logic, definition is not considered a report, but Ibn Taymiyya argues it functions like one.
5
Assess the exception and its implications
Ibn Taymiyya notes an exception: if the hearer already shares knowledge with the definer, or if the matter is about absent things (ghā'iba), then accepting the definition on authority (taqlīd) might be permissible. But this is not the case for basic concepts like 'man'.
This exception shows Ibn Taymiyya's nuance; he does not reject all definitions, only their claim to produce new conceptual knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: لو حصل تصور المحدود بالحد لحصل ذلك قبل العلم بصحة الحد فإنه دليل التصور وطريقه وكاشفه، فمن الممتنع أن نعلم صحة المعرف المحدود قبل العلم بصحة المعرف. والعلم بصحة الحد لا يحصل إلا بعد العلم بالمحدود، إذ الحد خبر عن مخبر هو المحدود، فمن الممتنع أن يعلم صحة الخبر وصدقه قبل تصور المخبر عنه من غير تقليد المخبر وقبول قوله فيما يشترك في العلم به المخبر والمخبر ليس هو من باب الأخبار عن الأمور الغائبة.
The third aspect: if conceptualization of the defined occurred through definition, that would happen before knowledge of the validity of the definition, because the definition is the evidence, path, and revealer of the conceptualization. So it is impossible to know the validity of the defined before knowing the validity of the definer. And knowledge of the validity of the definition only occurs after knowledge of the defined, since the definition is a report about an entity that is the defined. Therefore, it is impossible to know the validity and truth of the report before conceptualizing the reported-about, unless one is imitating the reporter and accepting his statement in matters where the reporter and the listener share in knowledge—and the reporter is not like reports about absent matters.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's third argument (الوجه الثالث) against the claim that definition (hadd) produces conceptual knowledge. He points out a circularity: if the definition is the means to conceptualize the defined, then one must first know the definition is correct before using it, but the correctness of the definition depends on already knowing the defined. Therefore, definition cannot be a source of new knowledge. He also notes that accepting the definer's statement without independent verification is like taqlid, which is not acceptable in matters of certainty. This argument relies on the principle that a report cannot be verified without prior knowledge of its subject. The example implicit here is that any definition, such as 'man is a rational animal', cannot be validated unless one already understands what 'man' is.

Ibn Taymiyya's Challenge: Definitions Presuppose Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya argues definition requires prior knowledge of essential attributes, then allows definition as reminder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definitions are epistemically redundant and identify his concession that they serve as reminders.

Core Idea
Definitions presuppose what they claim to teach.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that definitions (ḥudūd) produce knowledge of essences. He presents a dilemma: either the listener already knows the essential attributes (dhātiyya) included in the definition, or he does not. In either case, the definition cannot generate new conceptual knowledge. However, he allows that definitions can serve as reminders (tanbīh), directing attention to concepts already known. This lesson unpacks his fourth argument and its implications for the role of definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about essential attributes
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya describes the attributes called 'dhātiyya' (essential) and their role as 'parts of the definition' (ajzāʾ al-ḥadd). Understand that logicians claim these attributes constitute the essence of the defined thing.
2
Apply the dilemma: does the listener know the attributes?
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning: if the listener already knows the defined is described by those attributes, then he already conceptualizes it without the definition. If he does not know, he cannot understand the definition. Both horns lead to the conclusion that the definition does not produce new knowledge.
Consider the two suppositions: knowing vs. not knowing.
3
Evaluate the example: 'Man is a rational animal'
Work through the standard definition of man as 'rational animal'. If the listener doesn't know 'man', he must either already conceptualize 'rational animal' and know the relation, or not. In the first case, he needs to learn that 'man' refers to that concept (not a new concept). In the second case, he needs two concepts plus the relation. In neither case does the definition provide the concept of 'man' anew.
This example illustrates the redundancy of definition.
4
Understand the concession: definition as reminder (tanbīh)
Ibn Taymiyya concedes that a definition may remind (tanbīh) the mind of something already known. Just as a name (ism) can direct attention to a concept, a definition can turn the heedless mind toward the already-conceptualized thing.
Compare the function of a name vs. definition—both are triggers, not teachers.
5
Summarize the epistemological shift
Conclude that definition, according to Ibn Taymiyya, does not produce new conceptual knowledge but recalls what is already known. This redefines the role of definition from a tool of discovery to a tool of reminder.
This challenges the foundational claim of Aristotelian logic about definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع: أنهم يحدون المحدود بالصفات التي يسمونها «الذاتية»، ويسمونها أجزاء الحد» و«أجزاء الماهية» و«المقومة لها» و«الداخلة فيها»، ونحو ذلك من العبارات. فإن لم يعلم المستمع أن المحدود موصوف بتلك الصفات امتنع أن يتصوره، وإن علم أنه موصوف بها كان قد تصوره بدون الحد. فثبت أنه على تقدير النقيضين لا يكون قد تصوره بالحد وهذا بين. فإنه إذا قيل: «الإنسان» هو الحيوان الناطق، فإن لم يكن قد عرف «الإنسان» قبل هذا - بل كان متصوراً لمسمى «الحيوان الناطق) ولا يعلم أنه «الإنسان» - احتاج إلى العلم بهذه النسبة. وإن لم يكن متصوراً لمسمى «الحيوان الناطق» احتاج إلى شيئين: إلى تصور ذلك، وإلى العلم بالنسبة المذكورة. وإن عرف ذلك كان قد تصور «الإنسان» بدون الحد. نعم، الحد قد ينبه تنبيهاً على تصور المحدود، كما ينبه الاسم. فإن الذهن قد يكون اسمه أو حده أقبل بذهنه إلى الشيء الذي أشير إليه بالاسم غافلاً عن الشيء، فإذا سمع الاسم أو الحد، فيتصوره. فيكون فائدة الحد من جنس فائدة الاسم وهذا هو الصواب.
The fourth aspect: they define the defined by attributes which they call 'essential' (dhātiyya), and they call them 'parts of the definition', 'parts of the quiddity', 'constitutive of it', 'included in it', and similar expressions. If the listener does not know that the defined is described by those attributes, it is impossible for him to conceptualize it; and if he knows that it is described by them, then he had already conceptualized it without the definition. Thus it is established that on either supposition, he does not conceptualize it by the definition—this is clear. For if it is said: 'Man is a rational animal', then if the person did not know 'man' before this—but rather was conceptualizing the meaning of 'rational animal' and does not know that it is 'man'—he needs knowledge of this relation. And if he was not conceptualizing the meaning of 'rational animal', he needs two things: conceptualization of that and knowledge of the mentioned relation. And if he knew that, then he had already conceptualized 'man' without the definition. Yes, the definition may serve as a reminder to conceptualize the defined, just as the name reminds. For the mind may be heedless of the thing, and the name or definition may turn the mind toward the thing indicated by the name. So when he hears the name or definition, he conceptualizes it. Thus the benefit of definition is of the same kind as the benefit of the name—this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument (الوجه الرابع) against the utility of definition. He notes that definitions consist of essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-dhātiyya) such as genus and differentia. For the listener to understand the definition, either he already knows that the defined has those attributes (in which case the definition is redundant) or he does not (in which case the definition cannot help him because he lacks the requisite concepts). He illustrates with the standard example of 'man is a rational animal': if the listener does not already know 'man', he must either already know 'rational animal' as a concept (and then needs to link it to 'man') or not know 'rational animal' at all (and then needs two concepts). In either case, the definition does not provide new knowledge. However, Ibn Taymiyya concedes that a definition can serve as a reminder (tanbīh), similar to how a name can direct the mind to an already-known concept. This is the correct view: definition is not a source of new concepts but a tool for recall.

Key Terms
الذاتية
أجزاء الماهية
حيوان ناطق
تنبيه

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument shows that definitions cannot be a source of new conceptual knowledge; they can only remind of what is already known.

Definitions as Reminders: Ibn Taymiyya's Concession
Author concedes that a definition can serve to alert or remind the mind of a known thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's view that the function of a definition is limited to alerting (tanbih) like a name, and distinguish it from the logicians' claim that definitions produce new knowledge.

Core Idea
Definitions are reminders, not discoveries.

Ibn Taymiyya is famous for his critique of the logicians' epistemology. Here he makes a crucial concession: definitions can serve a function. But he insists that function is no different from that of a name—merely to alert the mind to something already known. This passage sets the stage for a radical reduction of the role of definitions in acquiring knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting concession
Note that Ibn Taymiyya begins with 'Yes' (na'm), indicating he is agreeing with a part of the logicians' claim. What exactly is he conceding? He concedes that a definition can alert or remind, just like a name.
This is a strategic concession: he does not concede that definitions give new knowledge.
2
Analyze the mechanism of alerting
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the mind may be 'heedless' (ghafla) of a thing it already knows. When it hears the name or the definition, it becomes aware again. This is a psychological process, not a logical one.
The key technical term here is tanbih (alerting), which is borrowed from everyday discourse.
3
Compare definition to name
He explicitly says the benefit (fa'ida) of a definition is 'of the same genus' (min jins) as the benefit of a name. Both serve to bring to mind what is known but momentarily forgotten.
This reduces the definition to a mere linguistic tool.
4
Extract the epistemological conclusion
Since the definition only reminds, it cannot produce new conceptual knowledge (tasawwur hadith). This directly opposes the logicians' view that hadd provides essential definitions that teach the reality of things.
This is the core of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the epistemological value of definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم، الحد قد ينبه تنبيهاً، كما ينبه الاسم. فإن الذهن قد يكون اسمه أو حده أقبل بذهنه إلى الشيء الذي أشير إليه بالاسم غافلا عن الشيء، فإذا سمع اسمه أو الحد، فيتصوره. فيكون فائدة الحد من جنس فائدة الاسم وهذا هو الصواب.
Yes, the definition may alert an alerting, just as the name alerts. For the mind may be more inclined by the name or the definition to the thing pointed to by the name, heedless of the thing. So when it hears its name or the definition, it conceives it. Thus the benefit of the definition is of the same genus as the benefit of the name, and this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents a concession (na'm) by Ibn Taymiyya to the logicians: a definition can indeed have a function, but it is merely that of alerting or reminding (tanbih), just like a name. He explains that the mind may be heedless of a known thing, and hearing either the name or the definition directs attention to it, causing conception. Therefore, the benefit of a definition is the same as that of a name: to bring to mind something already known, not to produce new knowledge. This is presented as the correct position.

C) To alert the mind to a thing it already knows but is heedless of.
D) To replace the name of the thing.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reduces the definition to the epistemic function of a name: reminding, not teaching.

Ibn Taymiyya's Fourth Argument: Definition Requires Prior Knowledge of the Defined
Author presents the fourth argument against the logical definition producing new knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument against the productivity of definitions and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
Definitions cannot produce new knowledge because they either presuppose the defined or fail to convey it.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) can give us new conceptual knowledge of a thing's essence. He presents a clever dilemma: to understand a definition, the listener must already know the defined, or else the definition is useless. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why it challenges a foundational assumption of classical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definitions
Read the first sentence: logicians say definitions consist of essential attributes (dhatiyya) that constitute the essence. They claim these definitions produce new knowledge (tasawwur) of the defined.
This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma
He presents two possibilities: either the listener knows that the defined possesses those attributes, or he does not. If he knows, he already had the conception without the definition. If he does not know, the definition cannot give it to him because he doesn't know what is being attributed to what.
The dilemma is exhaustive: both horns lead to the same conclusion.
3
Apply the dilemma to the example given
Consider defining 'human' as 'rational animal'. If the listener already understands 'rational animal' but doesn't know it's called 'human', he must learn a relation (that 'human' names this meaning) – not from the definition. If he doesn't understand 'rational animal', the definition is meaningless.
This illustrates the gap between conception and naming.
4
Evaluate the force of the critique
Ask: Does Ibn Taymiyya show that definitions never teach new concepts? Or only that they cannot be the primary means of acquiring concepts? Consider whether the logicians might respond that definitions clarify and organize already possessed knowledge.
Think critically: Is the argument decisive or limited?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أنهم يحدون المحدود بالصفات التي يسمونها «الذاتية»، ويسمونها «أجزاء الحد» و«أجزاء الماهية» و«المقومة لها» و«الداخلة فيها»، ونحو ذلك من العبارات. فإن لم يعلم المستمع أن المحدود موصوف بتلك الصفات امتنع أن يتصوره، وإن علم أنه موصوف بها كان قد تصوره بدون الحد. فثبت أنه على تقدير النقيضين لا يكون قد تصوره بالحد وهذا بين. فإنه إذا قيل: «الإنسان» هو الحيوان الناطق، فإن لم يكن قد عرف «الإنسان» قبل هذا - بل كان متصوراً لمسمى «الحيوان الناطق» ولا يعلم أنه «الإنسان» - احتاج إلى العلم بهذه النسبة. وإن لم يكن متصوراً لمسمى «الحيوان الناطق» احتاج إلى شيئين: إلى تصور ذلك، وإلى العلم بالنسبة المذكورة. وإن عرف ذلك كان قد تصور «الإنسان» بدون الحد.
Fourth: They define the defined by the attributes they call 'essential', and they call them 'parts of the definition', 'parts of the essence', 'the constitutive of it', and 'the internal to it', and similar expressions. If the listener does not know that the defined is described by those attributes, it is impossible for him to conceive it; and if he knows that it is described by them, then he already conceived it without the definition. So it is established that on both suppositions, he does not conceive it by the definition, and this is clear. For if it is said: 'Man' is the rational animal, if he did not know 'man' before this—rather he was conceiving the meaning of 'rational animal' and does not know that it is 'man'—he needs knowledge of this relation. And if he is not conceiving the meaning of 'rational animal', he needs two things: conception of that, and knowledge of the mentioned relation. And if he knows that, he conceived 'man' without the definition.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) produce new conceptual knowledge. The author argues that the definition either presupposes knowledge of the defined or fails to convey it. He states that logicians define the defined by attributes they call 'essential' and consider parts of the definition and essence. If the listener does not already know that the defined possesses these attributes, he cannot conceive it through the definition. If he does know, he already has the conception without the definition. Thus, on both possibilities, the definition does not produce new conceptual knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya illustrates with the example of defining 'human' (al-insan) as 'rational animal' (al-hayawan al-natiq). If the listener does not know what 'human' is but conceives 'rational animal', he must still learn the relation that 'human' is the name of that meaning—this is not provided by the definition. If he does not conceive 'rational animal', he must learn that too. In either case, the definition does not yield original knowledge.

المحدود
الذاتية

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument shows that definitions cannot be the source of new conceptual knowledge because they either presuppose or fail to convey the defined.

Ibn Taymiyya on Definition as Conventional Distinction
Author compares logical definitions to spatial boundaries, emphasizing distinction over essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definition as essence-revealing and distinguish between definition by attributes and definition by directions.

Core Idea
Definitions do not reveal essences; they only distinguish names.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya continues his radical rethinking of logical definition (hadd). He compares defining by attributes to defining by spatial boundaries, arguing that both serve only to distinguish one thing from another, not to reveal its inner nature. This sets the stage for his claim that definitions are conventional linguistic acts, varying by language, law, and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Ibn Taymiyya compares defining by attributes to defining by directions. Notice how both serve to mark a boundary that includes some things and excludes others, rather than describing an essence.
The land boundary example uses cardinal directions; attributes are like conceptual boundaries.
2
Extract the function of definition
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the true benefit (fā'ida) of definition. He says it explains the meaning of the name (bayān musammā al-ism) and distinguishes the defined from others (tamyīz al-mahdūd 'an ghayrihī), not a depiction (taṣwīr).
3
Recognize the two types of definitions
Ibn Taymiyya mentions verbal (lafziyya) and descriptive (wasfiyya) definitions. Both are merely explanations of the name, not essential definitions.
This rejects the logicians' distinction between real and verbal definitions.
4
Analyze the conventional nature
Since definition explains a name, and naming is conventional (wadʿī), definition depends on the intention of the namer and the linguistic background. Consider how this challenges universal essentialism.
5
Apply the jurists' classification
The passage ends with definitions divided by source: language, sacred law, custom. Think of a fiqh term that fits each category, such as 'ṣalāh' (law), 'bayʿ' (custom), 'rajul' (language).
This reinforces that definitions are context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحديد بالصفات كالتحديد بالجهات. كما إذا قيل: حد الأرض من الجانب القبلى كذا، ومن الجانب الشرقي كذا، وميزت الأرض باسمها وحدها. وحد الأرض يُحتاج إليه، إذا خيف من الزيادة في المسمى أو النقص منه، فيفيد إدخال المحدود جميعه وإخراج ما ليس منه، كما يفيد الاسم. وكذلك حد النوع. وهذا يحصل بالحدود اللفظية تارةً، وبالوصفية أخرى. وحقيقة الحد في الموضعين بيان مسمى الاسم فقط، وتمييز المحدود عن غيره، لا تصوير المحدود. وإذا كان فائدة الحد بيان مسمى الاسم والتسمية أمر لغوي وضعي، رجع في ذلك إلى قصد ذلك المسمي ولغته. ولهذا يقول الفقهاء: من الأسماء ما يعرف حده بـ«اللغة»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«الشرع»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«العرف».
Defining by attributes is like defining by directions. Just as if it is said: the boundary of the land from the southern side is such, and from the eastern side is such, and the land is distinguished by its name and its boundary. And the boundary of the land is needed if one fears increase or decrease in the named thing, so it benefits by including all of the defined and excluding what is not from it, just as the name benefits. And so too the boundary of the species. This is attained sometimes by verbal definitions and sometimes by descriptive definitions. And the reality of the definition in both cases is only an explanation of the meaning of the name and a distinction of the defined from others, not a depiction of the defined. And since the benefit of the definition is explanation of the meaning of the name, and naming is a conventional linguistic matter, one refers in that to the intention of that namer and his language. Hence the jurists say: among names are those whose definition is known by language, among them are those whose definition is known by sacred law, and among them are those whose definition is known by custom.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition of the function of definition (hadd). He compares defining by attributes (al-tahdid bi al-sifat) to defining by directions (al-tahdid bi al-jahat). Just as the boundaries of a piece of land are given by its cardinal directions to distinguish it from other lands, so definitions of species (naw') are given by attributes to include all individuals and exclude others. This is achieved either verbally (lafziyya) or descriptively (wasfiyya). The true nature of definition (haqiqat al-hadd) is simply to explain the meaning of the name (bayān musammā al-ism) and to distinguish the defined from others (tamyīz al-mahdūd 'an ghayrihī), not to depict its essence (taṣwīr al-mahdūd). Since naming is conventional (wad'i), definition must refer to the intention of the namer and the linguistic background. Ibn Taymiyya then cites the jurists' classification: some names are defined by language (lugha), some by sacred law (shar'), and some by custom ('urf). This reinforces that definitions are conventional, not essential.

Key Terms
التحديد بالصفات
الحدود اللفظية
حد النوع
