Definitions Only Distinguish, They Do Not Depict Essence
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions only distinguish, not convey essence, quoting Ghazali and Farabi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that definitions convey essence, and explain why he reduces definitions to a linguistic-legal function.

Core Idea
Definitions do not give you the essence – they only point to which name goes with which meaning.

In classical Islamic logic, definitions were considered the primary route to conception (tasawwur). Ibn Taymiyya turns this on its head: definitions are no different from names – they distinguish, not depict. Understanding this critique is crucial for evaluating the epistemological claims of Aristotelian logic in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The logicians claim definitions produce new conceptual knowledge of the essence. Ibn Taymiyya's passage directly challenges this.
2
Notice the analogy
He compares definitions of species to defining land by directional boundaries – both only distinguish, not reveal inner nature.
3
Extract the key phrase
The reality of the definition is solely the clarification of the meaning of the name, and the distinction of the defined from others, not the depiction of the defined.
4
Recognize the shift
He reduces hadd from an essential definition to a linguistic tool, undermining the logical tradition's core claim.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Therefore, definitions are not a source of certain knowledge; genuine conception comes from direct experience or analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحديد بالصفات كالتحديد بالجهات. وهو «التمييز بين الشيء المحدود وغيره». وتكون الحدود للأنواع بالصفات كالحدود للأعيان بالجهات. كما إذا قيل : حد الأرض من الجانب القبلى كذا، ومن الجانب الشرقي كذا، وميزت الأرض باسمها وحدها. وحد الأرض يُحتاج إليه، إذا خيف من الزيادة في المسمّى أو النقص منه، فيفيد إدخال المحدود جميعه وإخراج ما ليس منه، كما يفيد الاسم. وكذلك حد النوع. وهذا يحصل بالحدود اللفظية تارةً، وبالوصفية أخرى. وحقيقة الحدّ باللغة بيان مسمّى الاسم فقط، وتمييز المحدود عن غيره، لا تصوير المحدود. وإذا كان فائدة الحدّ بيان مسمّى الاسم والتسمية أمر لغوي وضعي، رُجع في ذلك إلى قصد ذلك المسمِّي ولغته. ولهذا يقول الفقهاء : من الأسماء ما يعرف حده بـ«اللغة»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«الشرع»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«العُرف». ومن هذا تفسيرُ الكلام وشرحه إذا أريد به تبيين مراد المتكلم؛ فهذا يُبنى على معرفة حدود كلامه وإذا أريد به تبيين صحته وتقريره فإنه يحتاج إلى معرفة دليل صحة الكلام. فالأول فيه بيان تصوير كلامه والثاني بيان تصديق كلامه. وتصوير كلامه كتصوير مسمّيات الأسماء بـ «الترجمة» تارةً لمن يكون قد تَصَوَّر المسمى ولم يعرف أن ذلك اسمه، وتارةً لمن لم يكن قد تَصَوَّر المسمى فيشار له إلى المسمى بحسب الإمكان - إما إلى عينه وإما إلى نظيره. ولهذا يقال : الحد تارة يكون لـ«الاسم»، وتارة يكون لـ«المسمى». وأئمة المصنفين في صناعة الحدود على طريقة المنطقيين يعترفون عند التحقيق بذلك، كما ذكر أبو حامد الغزالي في كتاب معيار العلم الذي صنفه في المنطق، بعد أن قال : البحث النظري الجاري في الطلب إما أن يتجه إلى «تصور» أو إلى «تصديق». فالموصل إلى «التصور» يسمى «قولاً شارحاً»، فمنه «حد» ومنه «رسم». والمُوصِلُ إلى «التصديق» يسمى «حُجّة»، فمنه «قياس» ومنه «استقراء» وغيره. قال الغزالي : مضمون هذا الكتاب تعريف مبادي القول «الشارح» لما أريد تصوره، «حداً» كان أو «رسماً»، وتعريف مبادي الحجة الموصلة إلى التصديق، «قياساً» كان أو غيره، مع التنبيه على شروط صحتها ومثار الغلط فيها. فإن قلت: كيف يجهل الإنسان العلم التصوّري حتى يفتقر إلى «الحد»؟ قلنا: بأن يسمع الإنسان اسماً لا يعرف معناه كمن قال: «ما الخَلاء؟» و«ما المُلَاء؟» و«ما الشيطان؟» و«ما العقار؟» فيقال: «العُقَار: الخمر. فإن لم يعرفه باسمه المعروف يفهمه بحده، فيقال: «الخمر» هو «شراب» مسكر، معتَصَر من العنب، فيحصل له علم تصوّري بذات «الخمر». قلت : فقد بَيَّن أن فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأن ذلك كمن سمع اسماً لا يعرف معناه فيذكر له اسم، فإن لم يتصوره وإلا ذكر له الحد. وهذا مما يعنون به حُذاقهم، حتى قال معلمهم الثاني أبو نصر الفارابي - وهو أعظم الفلاسفة كلاماً في المنطق وتفاريعه - قال في «كتابه» : ومعلوم أن الاسم لا يفيد بنفسه تصوير المسمّى، وإنما يفيد التمييز بينه وبين غيره. وأما تصوّر المسمّى فتارة يتصوّره الإنسان بذاته - بحسّه الباطن أو الظاهر، وتارةً يتصوّره بتصوره نظيره، وهو أبعد. فمن عرف عين «الخمر» إذا لم يعرف مسمّى لفظ «العُقار» قيل له : هو «الخمر» أو غيرها من الأسماء، فعرفها. ومن لم يعرف عين الخمر بحال عُرف بنظيرها، فقيل له : هو «شراب». فإذا تصوّر القدر المشترك بين النظيرين ذكر له ما يميزها، فقيل «مسكر».
Determination by attributes is like determination by directions. It is 'the distinction between the thing defined and others.' And the definitions of species are by attributes, like the definitions of physical objects by directions. For example, when it is said: the boundary of the land from the qibla side is such-and-such, and from the eastern side is such-and-such, and the land is distinguished by its name and its boundary. And the boundary of the land is needed if there is fear of increase in the named or decrease from it, so it benefits by including all of the defined and excluding what is not from it, just as the name benefits. And similarly the definition of the species. This occurs sometimes by verbal definitions, and sometimes by descriptive definitions. And the reality of the definition in language is solely the clarification of the meaning of the name, and the distinction of the defined from others, not the depiction of the defined. And since the benefit of the definition is clarifying the meaning of the name, and naming is a linguistic conventional matter, reference is made in that to the intention of that name-giver and his language. Because of this, the jurists say: among names are those whose definition is known by 'language,' among them those whose definition is known by 'divine law,' and among them those whose definition is known by 'custom.' And from this is the interpretation of speech and its explanation if what is intended is clarifying the speaker's intention; this is built upon knowledge of the boundaries of his speech. And if what is intended is clarifying its correctness and establishing it, then it needs knowledge of the proof of the correctness of the speech. The first involves clarification of the conception of his speech, the second involves clarification of the assent to his speech. And the conception of his speech is like the conception of the meanings of names, sometimes by 'translation' for one who has already conceived the meaning but did not know that this is its name, and sometimes for one who has not yet conceived the meaning, so he is pointed to the meaning according to possibility – either to its very self or to its likeness. Because of this it is said: the definition is sometimes of the 'name,' and sometimes of the 'named.' And the leading authors in the craft of definitions according to the method of the logicians admit this upon investigation, as Abu Hamid al-Ghazali mentioned in the book 'The Criterion of Knowledge' which he authored on logic, after he said: theoretical investigation pertaining to inquiry is either directed towards 'conception' or towards 'assent.' What leads to 'conception' is called 'explanatory statement,' which includes 'definition' and 'description.' And what leads to 'assent' is called 'proof,' which includes 'syllogism,' 'induction,' and others. Al-Ghazali said: The content of this book is the definition of the principles of the 'explanatory statement' for what is intended to be conceived, whether 'definition' or 'description,' and the definition of the principles of the proof that leads to assent, whether 'syllogism' or other, along with drawing attention to the conditions of its validity and the sources of error in it. If you say: How can a person be ignorant of conceptual knowledge so that he needs the 'definition'? We say: That a person hears a name whose meaning he does not know, like one who says: 'What is al-khala?' and 'What is al-mala?' and 'What is al-Shaytan?' and 'What is al-'uqar?' So it is said: 'Al-'uqar is wine.' If he does not know it by its known name, he understands it by its definition, so it is said: 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage, extracted from grapes,' so he obtains conceptual knowledge of the essence of 'wine.' I say: So he has clarified that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that this is like one who hears a name whose meaning he does not know, so he is reminded of a name; if he does not conceive it, then the definition is mentioned to him. And this is what their experts concern themselves with, until their second teacher Abu Nasr al-Farabi – who is the greatest of the philosophers in discourse on logic and its branches – said in his book: It is known that the name by itself does not benefit the conception of the named, but rather benefits the distinction between it and others. As for the conception of the named, sometimes a person conceives it by itself – through his inner or outer sense – and sometimes he conceives it by conceiving its likeness, which is more remote. So one who knows the very essence of 'wine' but does not know the meaning of the word 'al-'uqar' is told: it is 'wine' or other names, so he knows it. And one who does not know the essence of wine at all is made to know it by its likeness, so it is said to him: it is a 'beverage.' Then if he conceives the common factor between the two likenesses, he is told what distinguishes it, so it is said: 'intoxicating.'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logical tradition's view of definitions. He begins by analogizing definitions of species by attributes to definitions of physical territories by directions. Just as a land is distinguished by its name and its boundaries, a species is distinguished by its definition and its descriptors. The key claim is that the definition only distinguishes the defined from others; it does not depict or convey the essence (taswir) of the defined. He emphasizes that the benefit of a definition is merely to clarify what a name means, and that naming is a conventional linguistic act. To support this, he cites jurists who classify definitions of names into linguistic, legal, and customary categories. He then distinguishes two purposes for interpreting speech: clarifying the speaker's intention (which requires knowledge of definitions) and establishing the correctness of speech (which requires proof). Definitions belong to the first category, which pertains to conception (tasawwur). He quotes al-Ghazali from the Criterion of Knowledge, where Ghazali admits that the 'explanatory statement' (which includes definition) leads to conception, not certainty. Ibn Taymiyya then uses Ghazali's own example of someone asking about the word 'al-'uqar' (wine) to illustrate that a definition merely substitutes one name for another or adds a description, but genuine conception comes from direct experience or acquaintance. Finally, he quotes al-Farabi, who explicitly states that a name does not yield conception of the thing; it only distinguishes. Conception comes either through direct sensory experience or through analogy to something similar. For example, one who knows wine but not the word 'al-'uqar' is simply given the name 'wine'; one who does not know wine at all is given a description – 'beverage' and 'intoxicating' – which presupposes prior knowledge of those terms. Thus, definitions are not a source of new conceptual knowledge; they only clarify which name corresponds to which meaning already known in some form. This is a direct critique of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) are essential to produce certain knowledge.

Types of Definitions and the Purpose of Interpretation
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions only distinguish, not convey essence, quoting Ghazali and Farabi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of definitions (lughawi, shar'i, 'urfi) and explain Ibn Taymiyya's division of interpretation into tabyin al-murad and tabyin al-sihha.

Core Idea
Knowing whether a definition is linguistic, legal, or customary is a prerequisite for understanding the speaker's intention.

Ibn Taymiyya shows that definitions are not monolithic; they vary by context. He then ties this to the broader distinction between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) – the first requires knowing what is meant, the second requires proof. This lesson focuses on his classification of definitions and the two purposes of interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's reference to jurists
He quotes that definitions are of three types – linguistic, legal, customary. This shows that the meaning of a term depends on the convention of the speaker.
2
Understand the implication
The meaning of a term is not fixed by logic alone; it depends on the context – language, law, or custom. This is a direct challenge to the logicians' assumption of universal definitions.
3
Connect to interpretation
He distinguishes two purposes: clarifying the speaker's intention (needs definition) and establishing correctness (needs proof). The first pertains to conception, the second to assent.
4
Note the link to tasawwur and tasdiq
Conception is knowing what the speaker means; assent is judging the truth of the claim. Definitions serve only the first.
5
Realize the practical application
In fiqh and kalam, determining the definition of a term like 'kufr' requires knowing whether it is used linguistically, legally, or customarily. Ignoring this leads to errors.

التحديد بالصفات كالتحديد بالجهات. وهو «التمييز بين الشيء المحدود وغيره». وتكون الحدود للأنواع بالصفات كالحدود للأعيان بالجهات. كما إذا قيل : حد الأرض من الجانب القبلى كذا، ومن الجانب الشرقي كذا، وميزت الأرض باسمها وحدها. وحد الأرض يُحتاج إليه، إذا خيف من الزيادة في المسمّى أو النقص منه، فيفيد إدخال المحدود جميعه وإخراج ما ليس منه، كما يفيد الاسم. وكذلك حد النوع. وهذا يحصل بالحدود اللفظية تارةً، وبالوصفية أخرى. وحقيقة الحدّ باللغة بيان مسمّى الاسم فقط، وتمييز المحدود عن غيره، لا تصوير المحدود. وإذا كان فائدة الحدّ بيان مسمّى الاسم والتسمية أمر لغوي وضعي، رُجع في ذلك إلى قصد ذلك المسمِّي ولغته. ولهذا يقول الفقهاء : من الأسماء ما يعرف حده بـ«اللغة»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«الشرع»، ومنه ما يعرف حده بـ«العُرف». ومن هذا تفسيرُ الكلام وشرحه إذا أريد به تبيين مراد المتكلم؛ فهذا يُبنى على معرفة حدود كلامه وإذا أريد به تبيين صحته وتقريره فإنه يحتاج إلى معرفة دليل صحة الكلام. فالأول فيه بيان تصوير كلامه والثاني بيان تصديق كلامه. وتصوير كلامه كتصوير مسمّيات الأسماء بـ «الترجمة» تارةً لمن يكون قد تَصَوَّر المسمى ولم يعرف أن ذلك اسمه، وتارةً لمن لم يكن قد تَصَوَّر المسمى فيشار له إلى المسمى بحسب الإمكان - إما إلى عينه وإما إلى نظيره. ولهذا يقال : الحد تارة يكون لـ«الاسم»، وتارة يكون لـ«المسمى». وأئمة المصنفين في صناعة الحدود على طريقة المنطقيين يعترفون عند التحقيق بذلك، كما ذكر أبو حامد الغزالي في كتاب معيار العلم الذي صنفه في المنطق، بعد أن قال : البحث النظري الجاري في الطلب إما أن يتجه إلى «تصور» أو إلى «تصديق». فالموصل إلى «التصور» يسمى «قولاً شارحاً»، فمنه «حد» ومنه «رسم». والمُوصِلُ إلى «التصديق» يسمى «حُجّة»، فمنه «قياس» ومنه «استقراء» وغيره. قال الغزالي : مضمون هذا الكتاب تعريف مبادي القول «الشارح» لما أريد تصوره، «حداً» كان أو «رسماً»، وتعريف مبادي الحجة الموصلة إلى التصديق، «قياساً» كان أو غيره، مع التنبيه على شروط صحتها ومثار الغلط فيها. فإن قلت: كيف يجهل الإنسان العلم التصوّري حتى يفتقر إلى «الحد»؟ قلنا: بأن يسمع الإنسان اسماً لا يعرف معناه كمن قال: «ما الخَلاء؟» و«ما المُلَاء؟» و«ما الشيطان؟» و«ما العقار؟» فيقال: «العُقَار: الخمر. فإن لم يعرفه باسمه المعروف يفهمه بحده، فيقال: «الخمر» هو «شراب» مسكر، معتَصَر من العنب، فيحصل له علم تصوّري بذات «الخمر». قلت : فقد بَيَّن أن فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأن ذلك كمن سمع اسماً لا يعرف معناه فيذكر له اسم، فإن لم يتصوره وإلا ذكر له الحد. وهذا مما يعنون به حُذاقهم، حتى قال معلمهم الثاني أبو نصر الفارابي - وهو أعظم الفلاسفة كلاماً في المنطق وتفاريعه - قال في «كتابه» : ومعلوم أن الاسم لا يفيد بنفسه تصوير المسمّى، وإنما يفيد التمييز بينه وبين غيره. وأما تصوّر المسمّى فتارة يتصوّره الإنسان بذاته - بحسّه الباطن أو الظاهر، وتارةً يتصوّره بتصوره نظيره، وهو أبعد. فمن عرف عين «الخمر» إذا لم يعرف مسمّى لفظ «العُقار» قيل له : هو «الخمر» أو غيرها من الأسماء، فعرفها. ومن لم يعرف عين الخمر بحال عُرف بنظيرها، فقيل له : هو «شراب». فإذا تصوّر القدر المشترك بين النظيرين ذكر له ما يميزها، فقيل «مسكر».
Determination by attributes is like determination by directions. It is 'the distinction between the thing defined and others.' And the definitions of species are by attributes, like the definitions of physical objects by directions. For example, when it is said: the boundary of the land from the qibla side is such-and-such, and from the eastern side is such-and-such, and the land is distinguished by its name and its boundary. And the boundary of the land is needed if there is fear of increase in the named or decrease from it, so it benefits by including all of the defined and excluding what is not from it, just as the name benefits. And similarly the definition of the species. This occurs sometimes by verbal definitions, and sometimes by descriptive definitions. And the reality of the definition in language is solely the clarification of the meaning of the name, and the distinction of the defined from others, not the depiction of the defined. And since the benefit of the definition is clarifying the meaning of the name, and naming is a linguistic conventional matter, reference is made in that to the intention of that name-giver and his language. Because of this, the jurists say: among names are those whose definition is known by 'language,' among them those whose definition is known by 'divine law,' and among them those whose definition is known by 'custom.' And from this is the interpretation of speech and its explanation if what is intended is clarifying the speaker's intention; this is built upon knowledge of the boundaries of his speech. And if what is intended is clarifying its correctness and establishing it, then it needs knowledge of the proof of the correctness of the speech. The first involves clarification of the conception of his speech, the second involves clarification of the assent to his speech. And the conception of his speech is like the conception of the meanings of names, sometimes by 'translation' for one who has already conceived the meaning but did not know that this is its name, and sometimes for one who has not yet conceived the meaning, so he is pointed to the meaning according to possibility – either to its very self or to its likeness. Because of this it is said: the definition is sometimes of the 'name,' and sometimes of the 'named.' And the leading authors in the craft of definitions according to the method of the logicians admit this upon investigation, as Abu Hamid al-Ghazali mentioned in the book 'The Criterion of Knowledge' which he authored on logic, after he said: theoretical investigation pertaining to inquiry is either directed towards 'conception' or towards 'assent.' What leads to 'conception' is called 'explanatory statement,' which includes 'definition' and 'description.' And what leads to 'assent' is called 'proof,' which includes 'syllogism,' 'induction,' and others. Al-Ghazali said: The content of this book is the definition of the principles of the 'explanatory statement' for what is intended to be conceived, whether 'definition' or 'description,' and the definition of the principles of the proof that leads to assent, whether 'syllogism' or other, along with drawing attention to the conditions of its validity and the sources of error in it. If you say: How can a person be ignorant of conceptual knowledge so that he needs the 'definition'? We say: That a person hears a name whose meaning he does not know, like one who says: 'What is al-khala?' and 'What is al-mala?' and 'What is al-Shaytan?' and 'What is al-'uqar?' So it is said: 'Al-'uqar is wine.' If he does not know it by its known name, he understands it by its definition, so it is said: 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage, extracted from grapes,' so he obtains conceptual knowledge of the essence of 'wine.' I say: So he has clarified that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that this is like one who hears a name whose meaning he does not know, so he is reminded of a name; if he does not conceive it, then the definition is mentioned to him. And this is what their experts concern themselves with, until their second teacher Abu Nasr al-Farabi – who is the greatest of the philosophers in discourse on logic and its branches – said in his book: It is known that the name by itself does not benefit the conception of the named, but rather benefits the distinction between it and others. As for the conception of the named, sometimes a person conceives it by itself – through his inner or outer sense – and sometimes he conceives it by conceiving its likeness, which is more remote. So one who knows the very essence of 'wine' but does not know the meaning of the word 'al-'uqar' is told: it is 'wine' or other names, so he knows it. And one who does not know the essence of wine at all is made to know it by its likeness, so it is said to him: it is a 'beverage.' Then if he conceives the common factor between the two likenesses, he is told what distinguishes it, so it is said: 'intoxicating.'

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logical tradition's view of definitions. He begins by analogizing definitions of species by attributes to definitions of physical territories by directions. Just as a land is distinguished by its name and its boundaries, a species is distinguished by its definition and its descriptors. He then distinguishes two purposes for interpreting speech: clarifying the speaker's intention (which requires knowledge of definitions) and establishing the correctness of speech (which requires proof). Definitions belong to the first category, which pertains to conception (tasawwur). Ibn Taymiyya then uses Ghazali's own example of someone asking about the word 'al-'uqar' (wine) to illustrate that a definition merely substitutes one name for another or adds a description, but genuine conception comes from direct experience or acquaintance. The Core Thesis: Definitions Benefit Like Names
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' claim that definitions provide direct conceptual knowledge, arguing they only distinguish like names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's claim that definitions (hudud) serve the same function as names—to distinguish—and provide evidence from the passage.

Core Idea
Definitions are reminders, not revelations; they distinguish like names, not depict essences.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' elevation of definitions as the primary means of conceptualization. He argues that a definition merely identifies what is being talked about, just as a name does. He uses the example of someone hearing the word 'al-`uqaar' and being told it means 'wine' to demonstrate that definitions only provide distinguishing labels.

Guided Path
1
Locate the thesis
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya states that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as names. What is the exact Arabic phrase?
Look for 'فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء'.
2
Analyze the example
Work through the example of al-`uqaar and wine. How does the definition function? Does it give new conceptual knowledge or only identify?
Notice that the definition 'wine is an intoxicating beverage pressed from grapes' only works if the listener already knows 'intoxicating' and 'beverage'.
3
Interrogate al-Farabi's quote
What does al-Farabi say about names and conceptualization? How does Ibn Taymiyya use this to support his thesis?
Al-Farabi says names only give distinction, not a mental image.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Put together: how does this show that definitions are not superior to names?
The definition only reminds or distinguishes, just as a name does.

المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء

فالموصل إلى التصور يسمى قولا شارحا، فمنه حد ومنه رسم. والموصل إلى التصديق يسمى حجة، فمنه قياس ومنه استقراء وغيره.

قال الغزالي: مضمون هذا الكتاب تعريف مبادي القول الشارح لما أريد تصوره، حدا كان أو رسما، وتعريف مبادي الحجة الموصلة إلى التصديق، قياسا كان أو غيره، مع التنبيه على شروط صحتها ومثار الغلط فيها.

فإن قلت: كيف يجهل الإنسان العلم التصوري حتى يفتقر إلى الحد؟ قلنا: بأن يسمع الإنسان اسما لا يعرف معناه كمن قال: ما الخلاء؟ وما الملاء؟ وما الشيطان؟ وما العقار؟ فيقال: العقار: الخمر. فإن لم يعرفه باسمه المعروف يفهمه بحده، فيقال: الخمر هو شراب مسكر، معتصر من العنب، فيحصل له علم تصوري بذات الخمر. قلت: فقد بين أن فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأن ذلك كمن سمع اسما لا يعرف معناه فيذكر له اسم، فإن لم يتصوره وإلا ذكر له الحد. وهذا مما يعنون به حذاقهم، حتى قال معلمهم الثاني أبو نصر الفارابي - وهو أعظم الفلاسفة كلاما في المنطق وتفاريعه - قال في كتابه: ومعلوم أن الاسم لا يفيد بنفسه تصوير المسمى، وإنما يفيد التمييز بينه وبين غيره. وأما تصور المسمى فتارة يتصوره الإنسان بذاته - بحسه الباطن أو الظاهر، وتارة يتصوره بتصوره نظيره، وهو أبعد. فمن عرف عين الخمر إذا لم يعرف مسمى لفظ العقار قيل له: هو الخمر أو غيرها من الأسماء، فعرفها. ومن لم يعرف عين الخمر بحال عرف بنظيرها، فقيل له: هو شراب. فإذا تصور القدر المشترك بين النظيرين ذكر له ما يميزها، فقيل مسكر.

ثم قال: ولكن الكلام في تصوره لمعنى المسكر كالكلام في تصوره لمعنى الخمر. فإن لم يعرف عين المسكر وإلا لم يمكن تعريفه إلا بنظيره، فيقال: هو زوال العقل. وهذا جنس يشترك فيه النوم والجنون والإغماء والسكر. فلا بد أن يميز السكر فيقال زوال العقل بما يلتذ به. ثم اللذة لا بد أن يكون قد تصور جنسها بالأكل والشرب وغيرهما.

وهذا يبين أن فائدة الحدود قد تكون أضعف من فائدة الأسماء، لأنها تفيد معرفة الشيء بنظيره، والاسم يكتفي به من عرفه بنفسه.

وحقيقة الأمر أن الحد هو أن تصف المحدود بما تفصل به بينه وبين غيره. والصفات تفيد معرفة الموصوف خبرا، وليس المخبر كالمعاين، ولا من عرف المشهود عليه بعينه كمن عرفه بصفته وحليته. فمن عرف المسمى بعينه كان الاسم مغنيا عن الحد كما تقدم. ومن لم يعرفه بعينه لم يفده الحد ما يفيد الاسم لمن عرفه بعينه، إذ الاسم هناك يدل على العين التي عرفها بنفسها. والحد لمن لم يعرف العين إنما يفيد معرفة النوع، لا معرفة العين، كما يتصور اللذة بشرب الخمر من لم يشربها قياسا على اللذة بالخبز واللحم، ومعلوم فرق ما بين اللذتين.

وليس مقصودنا أن فائدة الحدود أضعف مطلقا، وإنما المقصود أنها من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأنها مذكرة لا مصورة؛ أو معرفة بالتسمية، مميزة للمسمى من غيره؛ أو معرفة بالقياس.
The second station - the fourth aspect: The benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names.

That which leads to conceptualization is called an explanatory statement, and of it are definition and description. And that which leads to assent is called a proof, and of it are syllogism, induction, and others.

Al-Ghazali said: The content of this book is the definition of the principles of the explanatory statement for what is intended to be conceived, whether definition or description, and the definition of the principles of the proof leading to assent, whether syllogism or otherwise, along with alerting to the conditions of their validity and the sources of error in them.

If you say: How can a person be ignorant of conceptual knowledge so that he needs the definition? We say: By a person hearing a name whose meaning he does not know, like one who says: What is al-khala? What is al-mala? What is al-shaytan? What is al-`uqaar? So it is said: al-`uqaar is wine. If he does not know it by its well-known name, he understands it by its definition, so it is said: Wine is an intoxicating beverage, pressed from grapes, so he obtains conceptual knowledge of the essence of wine. I say: So it has been shown that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that this is like one who heard a name whose meaning he does not know and is reminded of another name; if he does not conceive it, then the definition is given to him. And this is what their proficient ones mean, until their second teacher Abu Nasr al-Farabi—and he is the most knowledgeable of the philosophers in logic and its branches—said in his book: It is known that the name by itself does not give an image of the named thing, but only gives distinction between it and others. As for the image of the named thing, sometimes a person imagines it by itself—by his inner or outer sense—and sometimes he imagines it by imagining its analogue, which is more distant. So whoever knows the essence of wine but does not know the meaning of the word 'al-`uqaar', it is said to him: It is wine or other names, so he knows it. And whoever does not know the essence of wine at all is made to know by its analogue, so it is said: It is a beverage. So when he conceives the common element between the two analogues, he is told what distinguishes them, so it is said: intoxicating.

Then he said: But the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'intoxicant' is like the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'wine'. For if he does not know the essence of the intoxicant, it cannot be defined except by its analogue, so it is said: It is the removal of intellect. And this is a genus in which sleep, madness, fainting, and intoxication share. So it is necessary to distinguish intoxication, and it is said: Removal of intellect by something pleasurable. Then pleasure must have its genus conceived by eating, drinking, and others.

And this shows that the benefit of definitions may be weaker than the benefit of names, because they give knowledge of a thing by its analogue, while the name suffices for the one who knows it by itself.

And the reality of the matter is that the definition is that you describe the defined thing by what distinguishes it from others. And descriptions give knowledge of the described thing as a report, and the reported is not like the seen, and the one who knows the witnessed thing by its essence is not like the one who knows it by its attribute and description. So whoever knows the named thing by its essence, the name suffices from the definition as stated. And whoever does not know it by its essence, the definition does not give him what the name gives to the one who knows it by its essence, for the name there indicates the essence that he knew by itself. And the definition for one who does not know the essence only gives knowledge of the type, not knowledge of the essence, just as one who has not drunk wine imagines the pleasure of drinking wine by analogy to the pleasure of bread and meat, and the difference between the two pleasures is known.

And it is not our intent that the benefit of definitions is absolutely weaker, rather the intent is that it is of the same genre as the benefit of names, and that it is a reminder, not a depiction; or a knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named from others; or a knowledge by analogy.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide direct conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). He argues that definitions serve the same function as names: they only distinguish one thing from another and do not convey the essence itself.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by categorizing statements: those leading to conceptualization are called explanatory statements (qawl sharh), which include definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm); those leading to assent (tasdiq) are called proofs (hujja), which include syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and others. He then quotes al-Ghazali's framing of the book's purpose: to explain the principles of both explanatory statements and proofs, including validity conditions.

He poses an objection: how can someone need a definition? He answers with an analogy: hearing a foreign name like 'al-`uqaar' without knowing its meaning. The logician says that if a person does not know the name, they can be given a more familiar name (e.g., 'khamr'), and if that fails, they are given a definition (e.g., 'wine is an intoxicating beverage pressed from grapes'). This demonstrates that a definition only provides knowledge similar to a name—it identifies what is meant, not the essence directly.

Ibn Taymiyya then cites al-Farabi, who asserts that a name only distinguishes the named thing from others; the actual conceptualization comes either from direct sensory experience or from analogy with something similar. For example, if someone knows the essence of wine but not the word 'al-`uqaar', the name 'wine' suffices. If they have never experienced wine, they are told it is a 'beverage' (a genus shared with other drinks) and then distinguished by it being 'intoxicating'.

This leads to a regress: defining 'intoxicant' requires defining 'intoxication' as 'removal of the intellect' (zawal al-`aql), which is a genus shared with sleep, madness, fainting, etc. It must be further specified as 'removal of the intellect by something pleasurable'. But then 'pleasure' must itself be defined by analogy with eating and drinking. Ibn Taymiyya points out that this chain shows definitions are often weaker than names, because they only provide knowledge by analogy, whereas names suffice for direct knowers.

He then states the real nature of definition: it is a description distinguishing the defined from other things. Descriptions give knowledge by report (khabar), not direct sight (mu`ayana). Knowing a person by description is different from knowing them by sight. Therefore, for someone who knows the essence directly, the name is sufficient; for someone who does not, the definition provides only knowledge of the type (naw')—like imagining the pleasure of wine through the pleasure of bread—not the individual essence (ayn).

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that he is not claiming definitions are absolutely weaker; rather, they are of the same genre as names: they remind, not depict; they provide knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named; or they provide knowledge by analogy. Thus, definitions do not produce new conceptual knowledge; they only help identify what is being referred to, just as names do.

The Regress of Definitions: An Infinite Chain
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' claim that definitions provide direct conceptual knowledge, arguing they only distinguish like names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace Ibn Taymiyya's regress argument showing that definitions lead to an infinite chain of analogical definitions.

Core Idea
Every definition relies on prior knowledge; ultimately, we end up at direct experience.

Ibn Taymiyya, citing al-Farabi, demonstrates that defining something like 'wine' leads to a chain: wine is an intoxicating beverage, intoxicant means removal of intellect, removal of intellect needs to be specified by 'by something pleasurable', and pleasure itself needs definition. This shows that definitions do not ground knowledge but merely push it back to direct acquaintance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting definition
What is the first definition given for al-`uqaar? Write it out: 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage pressed from grapes.'
This definition contains a genus (beverage) and differentiae (intoxicating, pressed from grapes).
2
Trace the next step
When 'intoxicating' (muskir) is unknown, how is it defined? Look at the passage: 'It is the removal of intellect (zawal al-`aql).'
This definition itself has a genus (removal of intellect) shared by sleep, madness, fainting, and intoxication.
3
Recognize the genus-species structure
Note that each definition adds a differentia to a broader genus. For intoxication, the differentia is 'by something pleasurable' (bi-ma yultadhdhu bihi).
This pattern mirrors the structure of definitions in logic.
4
Observe the regress
Why does this chain not terminate in a foundational definition? Because each new term (e.g., pleasure) also requires definition by analogy.
Ibn Taymiyya points out that we eventually return to direct sensible experience.
5
Apply the lesson
How does this challenge the logicians' view that definitions provide certain conceptual knowledge?
Definitions are dependent on prior concepts; they do not ground knowledge absolutely.

المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء

فالموصل إلى التصور يسمى قولا شارحا، فمنه حد ومنه رسم. والموصل إلى التصديق يسمى حجة، فمنه قياس ومنه استقراء وغيره.

قال الغزالي: مضمون هذا الكتاب تعريف مبادي القول الشارح لما أريد تصوره، حدا كان أو رسما، وتعريف مبادي الحجة الموصلة إلى التصديق، قياسا كان أو غيره، مع التنبيه على شروط صحتها ومثار الغلط فيها.

فإن قلت: كيف يجهل الإنسان العلم التصوري حتى يفتقر إلى الحد؟ قلنا: بأن يسمع الإنسان اسما لا يعرف معناه كمن قال: ما الخلاء؟ وما الملاء؟ وما الشيطان؟ وما العقار؟ فيقال: العقار: الخمر. فإن لم يعرفه باسمه المعروف يفهمه بحده، فيقال: الخمر هو شراب مسكر، معتصر من العنب، فيحصل له علم تصوري بذات الخمر. قلت: فقد بين أن فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأن ذلك كمن سمع اسما لا يعرف معناه فيذكر له اسم، فإن لم يتصوره وإلا ذكر له الحد. وهذا مما يعنون به حذاقهم، حتى قال معلمهم الثاني أبو نصر الفارابي - وهو أعظم الفلاسفة كلاما في المنطق وتفاريعه - قال في كتابه: ومعلوم أن الاسم لا يفيد بنفسه تصوير المسمى، وإنما يفيد التمييز بينه وبين غيره. وأما تصور المسمى فتارة يتصوره الإنسان بذاته - بحسه الباطن أو الظاهر، وتارة يتصوره بتصوره نظيره، وهو أبعد. فمن عرف عين الخمر إذا لم يعرف مسمى لفظ العقار قيل له: هو الخمر أو غيرها من الأسماء، فعرفها. ومن لم يعرف عين الخمر بحال عرف بنظيرها، فقيل له: هو شراب. فإذا تصور القدر المشترك بين النظيرين ذكر له ما يميزها، فقيل مسكر.

ثم قال: ولكن الكلام في تصوره لمعنى المسكر كالكلام في تصوره لمعنى الخمر. فإن لم يعرف عين المسكر وإلا لم يمكن تعريفه إلا بنظيره، فيقال: هو زوال العقل. وهذا جنس يشترك فيه النوم والجنون والإغماء والسكر. فلا بد أن يميز السكر فيقال زوال العقل بما يلتذ به. ثم اللذة لا بد أن يكون قد تصور جنسها بالأكل والشرب وغيرهما.

وهذا يبين أن فائدة الحدود قد تكون أضعف من فائدة الأسماء، لأنها تفيد معرفة الشيء بنظيره، والاسم يكتفي به من عرفه بنفسه.

وحقيقة الأمر أن الحد هو أن تصف المحدود بما تفصل به بينه وبين غيره. والصفات تفيد معرفة الموصوف خبرا، وليس المخبر كالمعاين، ولا من عرف المشهود عليه بعينه كمن عرفه بصفته وحليته. فمن عرف المسمى بعينه كان الاسم مغنيا عن الحد كما تقدم. ومن لم يعرفه بعينه لم يفده الحد ما يفيد الاسم لمن عرفه بعينه، إذ الاسم هناك يدل على العين التي عرفها بنفسها. والحد لمن لم يعرف العين إنما يفيد معرفة النوع، لا معرفة العين، كما يتصور اللذة بشرب الخمر من لم يشربها قياسا على اللذة بالخبز واللحم، ومعلوم فرق ما بين اللذتين.

وليس مقصودنا أن فائدة الحدود أضعف مطلقا، وإنما المقصود أنها من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأنها مذكرة لا مصورة؛ أو معرفة بالتسمية، مميزة للمسمى من غيره؛ أو معرفة بالقياس.
The second station - the fourth aspect: The benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names.

That which leads to conceptualization is called an explanatory statement, and of it are definition and description. And that which leads to assent is called a proof, and of it are syllogism, induction, and others.

Al-Ghazali said: The content of this book is the definition of the principles of the explanatory statement for what is intended to be conceived, whether definition or description, and the definition of the principles of the proof leading to assent, whether syllogism or otherwise, along with alerting to the conditions of their validity and the sources of error in them.

If you say: How can a person be ignorant of conceptual knowledge so that he needs the definition? We say: By a person hearing a name whose meaning he does not know, like one who says: What is al-khala? What is al-mala? What is al-shaytan? What is al-`uqaar? So it is said: al-`uqaar is wine. If he does not know it by its well-known name, he understands it by its definition, so it is said: Wine is an intoxicating beverage, pressed from grapes, so he obtains conceptual knowledge of the essence of wine. I say: So it has been shown that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that this is like one who heard a name whose meaning he does not know and is reminded of another name; if he does not conceive it, then the definition is given to him. And this is what their proficient ones mean, until their second teacher Abu Nasr al-Farabi—and he is the most knowledgeable of the philosophers in logic and its branches—said in his book: It is known that the name by itself does not give an image of the named thing, but only gives distinction between it and others. As for the image of the named thing, sometimes a person imagines it by itself—by his inner or outer sense—and sometimes he imagines it by imagining its analogue, which is more distant. So whoever knows the essence of wine but does not know the meaning of the word 'al-`uqaar', it is said to him: It is wine or other names, so he knows it. And whoever does not know the essence of wine at all is made to know by its analogue, so it is said: It is a beverage. So when he conceives the common element between the two analogues, he is told what distinguishes them, so it is said: intoxicating.

Then he said: But the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'intoxicant' is like the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'wine'. For if he does not know the essence of the intoxicant, it cannot be defined except by its analogue, so it is said: It is the removal of intellect. And this is a genus in which sleep, madness, fainting, and intoxication share. So it is necessary to distinguish intoxication, and it is said: Removal of intellect by something pleasurable. Then pleasure must have its genus conceived by eating, drinking, and others.

And this shows that the benefit of definitions may be weaker than the benefit of names, because they give knowledge of a thing by its analogue, while the name suffices for the one who knows it by itself.

And the reality of the matter is that the definition is that you describe the defined thing by what distinguishes it from others. And descriptions give knowledge of the described thing as a report, and the reported is not like the seen, and the one who knows the witnessed thing by its essence is not like the one who knows it by its attribute and description. So whoever knows the named thing by its essence, the name suffices from the definition as stated. And whoever does not know it by its essence, the definition does not give him what the name gives to the one who knows it by its essence, for the name there indicates the essence that he knew by itself. And the definition for one who does not know the essence only gives knowledge of the type, not knowledge of the essence, just as one who has not drunk wine imagines the pleasure of drinking wine by analogy to the pleasure of bread and meat, and the difference between the two pleasures is known.

And it is not our intent that the benefit of definitions is absolutely weaker, rather the intent is that it is of the same genre as the benefit of names, and that it is a reminder, not a depiction; or a knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named from others; or a knowledge by analogy.

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide direct conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). He argues that definitions serve the same function as names: they only distinguish one thing from another and do not convey the essence itself.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by categorizing statements: those leading to conceptualization are called explanatory statements (qawl sharh), which include definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm); those leading to assent (tasdiq) are called proofs (hujja), which include syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and others. He then quotes al-Ghazali's framing of the book's purpose: to explain the principles of both explanatory statements and proofs, including validity conditions.

He poses an objection: how can someone need a definition? He answers with an analogy: hearing a foreign name like 'al-`uqaar' without knowing its meaning. The logician says that if a person does not know the name, they can be given a more familiar name (e.g., 'khamr'), and if that fails, they are given a definition (e.g., 'wine is an intoxicating beverage pressed from grapes'). This demonstrates that a definition only provides knowledge similar to a name—it identifies what is meant, not the essence directly.

Ibn Taymiyya then cites al-Farabi, who asserts that a name only distinguishes the named thing from others; the actual conceptualization comes either from direct sensory experience or from analogy with something similar. For example, if someone knows the essence of wine but not the word 'al-`uqaar', the name 'wine' suffices. If they have never experienced wine, they are told it is a 'beverage' (a genus shared with other drinks) and then distinguished by it being 'intoxicating'.

This leads to a regress: defining 'intoxicant' requires defining 'intoxication' as 'removal of the intellect' (zawal al-`aql), which is a genus shared with sleep, madness, fainting, etc. It must be further specified as 'removal of the intellect by something pleasurable'. But then 'pleasure' must itself be defined by analogy with eating and drinking. Ibn Taymiyya points out that this chain shows definitions are often weaker than names, because they only provide knowledge by analogy, whereas names suffice for direct knowers.

He then states the real nature of definition: it is a description distinguishing the defined from other things. Descriptions give knowledge by report (khabar), not direct sight (mu`ayana). Knowing a person by description is different from knowing them by sight. Therefore, for someone who knows the essence directly, the name is sufficient; for someone who does not, the definition provides only knowledge of the type (naw')—like imagining the pleasure of wine through the pleasure of bread—not the individual essence (ayn).

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that he is not claiming definitions are absolutely weaker; rather, they are of the same genre as names: they remind, not depict; they provide knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named; or they provide knowledge by analogy. Thus, definitions do not produce new conceptual knowledge; they only help identify what is being referred to, just as names do.

Knowing the Essence vs. Knowing the Type
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' claim that definitions provide direct conceptual knowledge, arguing they only distinguish like names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between knowledge of the individual essence ('ayn) and knowledge of the type (naw') according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Definitions give knowledge of the type, not the concrete essence; only direct experience gives knowledge of the 'ayn.

Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between knowing something by its essence ('ayn) and knowing it by description or type (naw'). Definitions, he argues, only provide the latter. For example, someone who has never drunk wine can imagine its pleasure by analogy with other pleasures, but this is not the same as knowing wine's pleasure directly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
What does 'ayn' mean? What does 'naw'' mean? In the passage, 'ayn refers to the concrete individual essence, while naw' refers to the general type or species.
'Ayn is known by direct sensory or inner experience.
2
Apply to the wine example
Compare the knowledge of someone who has drunk wine (knows the 'ayn) to someone who only knows wine by its definition. According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does the latter lack?
The definer only knows the type (naw')—the common features shared with other beverages and intoxicants.
3
Examine the passage's concluding statement
Find the statement: 'The definition for one who does not know the essence only gives knowledge of the type, not knowledge of the essence.' What evidence does Ibn Taymiyya give?
He uses the analogy of imagining wine's pleasure through other pleasures.
4
Generalize
In what other Islamic science contexts is this distinction important? Consider knowing Allah's attributes.
Our knowledge of Allah is through names and attributes, not direct essence.

المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء

فالموصل إلى التصور يسمى قولا شارحا، فمنه حد ومنه رسم. والموصل إلى التصديق يسمى حجة، فمنه قياس ومنه استقراء وغيره.

قال الغزالي: مضمون هذا الكتاب تعريف مبادي القول الشارح لما أريد تصوره، حدا كان أو رسما، وتعريف مبادي الحجة الموصلة إلى التصديق، قياسا كان أو غيره، مع التنبيه على شروط صحتها ومثار الغلط فيها.

فإن قلت: كيف يجهل الإنسان العلم التصوري حتى يفتقر إلى الحد؟ قلنا: بأن يسمع الإنسان اسما لا يعرف معناه كمن قال: ما الخلاء؟ وما الملاء؟ وما الشيطان؟ وما العقار؟ فيقال: العقار: الخمر. فإن لم يعرفه باسمه المعروف يفهمه بحده، فيقال: الخمر هو شراب مسكر، معتصر من العنب، فيحصل له علم تصوري بذات الخمر. قلت: فقد بين أن فائدة الحدود من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأن ذلك كمن سمع اسما لا يعرف معناه فيذكر له اسم، فإن لم يتصوره وإلا ذكر له الحد. وهذا مما يعنون به حذاقهم، حتى قال معلمهم الثاني أبو نصر الفارابي - وهو أعظم الفلاسفة كلاما في المنطق وتفاريعه - قال في كتابه: ومعلوم أن الاسم لا يفيد بنفسه تصوير المسمى، وإنما يفيد التمييز بينه وبين غيره. وأما تصور المسمى فتارة يتصوره الإنسان بذاته - بحسه الباطن أو الظاهر، وتارة يتصوره بتصوره نظيره، وهو أبعد. فمن عرف عين الخمر إذا لم يعرف مسمى لفظ العقار قيل له: هو الخمر أو غيرها من الأسماء، فعرفها. ومن لم يعرف عين الخمر بحال عرف بنظيرها، فقيل له: هو شراب. فإذا تصور القدر المشترك بين النظيرين ذكر له ما يميزها، فقيل مسكر.

ثم قال: ولكن الكلام في تصوره لمعنى المسكر كالكلام في تصوره لمعنى الخمر. فإن لم يعرف عين المسكر وإلا لم يمكن تعريفه إلا بنظيره، فيقال: هو زوال العقل. وهذا جنس يشترك فيه النوم والجنون والإغماء والسكر. فلا بد أن يميز السكر فيقال زوال العقل بما يلتذ به. ثم اللذة لا بد أن يكون قد تصور جنسها بالأكل والشرب وغيرهما.

وهذا يبين أن فائدة الحدود قد تكون أضعف من فائدة الأسماء، لأنها تفيد معرفة الشيء بنظيره، والاسم يكتفي به من عرفه بنفسه.

وحقيقة الأمر أن الحد هو أن تصف المحدود بما تفصل به بينه وبين غيره. والصفات تفيد معرفة الموصوف خبرا، وليس المخبر كالمعاين، ولا من عرف المشهود عليه بعينه كمن عرفه بصفته وحليته. فمن عرف المسمى بعينه كان الاسم مغنيا عن الحد كما تقدم. ومن لم يعرفه بعينه لم يفده الحد ما يفيد الاسم لمن عرفه بعينه، إذ الاسم هناك يدل على العين التي عرفها بنفسها. والحد لمن لم يعرف العين إنما يفيد معرفة النوع، لا معرفة العين، كما يتصور اللذة بشرب الخمر من لم يشربها قياسا على اللذة بالخبز واللحم، ومعلوم فرق ما بين اللذتين.

وليس مقصودنا أن فائدة الحدود أضعف مطلقا، وإنما المقصود أنها من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأنها مذكرة لا مصورة؛ أو معرفة بالتسمية، مميزة للمسمى من غيره؛ أو معرفة بالقياس.
The second station - the fourth aspect: The benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names.

That which leads to conceptualization is called an explanatory statement, and of it are definition and description. And that which leads to assent is called a proof, and of it are syllogism, induction, and others.

Al-Ghazali said: The content of this book is the definition of the principles of the explanatory statement for what is intended to be conceived, whether definition or description, and the definition of the principles of the proof leading to assent, whether syllogism or otherwise, along with alerting to the conditions of their validity and the sources of error in them.

If you say: How can a person be ignorant of conceptual knowledge so that he needs the definition? We say: By a person hearing a name whose meaning he does not know, like one who says: What is al-khala? What is al-mala? What is al-shaytan? What is al-`uqaar? So it is said: al-`uqaar is wine. If he does not know it by its well-known name, he understands it by its definition, so it is said: Wine is an intoxicating beverage, pressed from grapes, so he obtains conceptual knowledge of the essence of wine. I say: So it has been shown that the benefit of definitions is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that this is like one who heard a name whose meaning he does not know and is reminded of another name; if he does not conceive it, then the definition is given to him. And this is what their proficient ones mean, until their second teacher Abu Nasr al-Farabi—and he is the most knowledgeable of the philosophers in logic and its branches—said in his book: It is known that the name by itself does not give an image of the named thing, but only gives distinction between it and others. As for the image of the named thing, sometimes a person imagines it by itself—by his inner or outer sense—and sometimes he imagines it by imagining its analogue, which is more distant. So whoever knows the essence of wine but does not know the meaning of the word 'al-`uqaar', it is said to him: It is wine or other names, so he knows it. And whoever does not know the essence of wine at all is made to know by its analogue, so it is said: It is a beverage. So when he conceives the common element between the two analogues, he is told what distinguishes them, so it is said: intoxicating.

Then he said: But the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'intoxicant' is like the discussion about his conception of the meaning of 'wine'. For if he does not know the essence of the intoxicant, it cannot be defined except by its analogue, so it is said: It is the removal of intellect. And this is a genus in which sleep, madness, fainting, and intoxication share. So it is necessary to distinguish intoxication, and it is said: Removal of intellect by something pleasurable. Then pleasure must have its genus conceived by eating, drinking, and others.

And this shows that the benefit of definitions may be weaker than the benefit of names, because they give knowledge of a thing by its analogue, while the name suffices for the one who knows it by itself.

And the reality of the matter is that the definition is that you describe the defined thing by what distinguishes it from others. And descriptions give knowledge of the described thing as a report, and the reported is not like the seen, and the one who knows the witnessed thing by its essence is not like the one who knows it by its attribute and description. So whoever knows the named thing by its essence, the name suffices from the definition as stated. And whoever does not know it by its essence, the definition does not give him what the name gives to the one who knows it by its essence, for the name there indicates the essence that he knew by itself. And the definition for one who does not know the essence only gives knowledge of the type, not knowledge of the essence, just as one who has not drunk wine imagines the pleasure of drinking wine by analogy to the pleasure of bread and meat, and the difference between the two pleasures is known.

And it is not our intent that the benefit of definitions is absolutely weaker, rather the intent is that it is of the same genre as the benefit of names, and that it is a reminder, not a depiction; or a knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named from others; or a knowledge by analogy.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide direct conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). He argues that definitions serve the same function as names: they only distinguish one thing from another and do not convey the essence itself.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by categorizing statements: those leading to conceptualization are called explanatory statements (qawl sharh), which include definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm); those leading to assent (tasdiq) are called proofs (hujja), which include syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and others. He then quotes al-Ghazali's framing of the book's purpose: to explain the principles of both explanatory statements and proofs, including validity conditions.

He poses an objection: how can someone need a definition? He answers with an analogy: hearing a foreign name like 'al-`uqaar' without knowing its meaning. The logician says that if a person does not know the name, they can be given a more familiar name (e.g., 'khamr'), and if that fails, they are given a definition (e.g., 'wine is an intoxicating beverage pressed from grapes'). This demonstrates that a definition only provides knowledge similar to a name—it identifies what is meant, not the essence directly.

Ibn Taymiyya then cites al-Farabi, who asserts that a name only distinguishes the named thing from others; the actual conceptualization comes either from direct sensory experience or from analogy with something similar. For example, if someone knows the essence of wine but not the word 'al-`uqaar', the name 'wine' suffices. If they have never experienced wine, they are told it is a 'beverage' (a genus shared with other drinks) and then distinguished by it being 'intoxicating'.

This leads to a regress: defining 'intoxicant' requires defining 'intoxication' as 'removal of the intellect' (zawal al-`aql), which is a genus shared with sleep, madness, fainting, etc. It must be further specified as 'removal of the intellect by something pleasurable'. But then 'pleasure' must itself be defined by analogy with eating and drinking. Ibn Taymiyya points out that this chain shows definitions are often weaker than names, because they only provide knowledge by analogy, whereas names suffice for direct knowers.

He then states the real nature of definition: it is a description distinguishing the defined from other things. Descriptions give knowledge by report (khabar), not direct sight (mu`ayana). Knowing a person by description is different from knowing them by sight. Therefore, for someone who knows the essence directly, the name is sufficient; for someone who does not, the definition provides only knowledge of the type (naw')—like imagining the pleasure of wine through the pleasure of bread—not the individual essence (ayn).

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that he is not claiming definitions are absolutely weaker; rather, they are of the same genre as names: they remind, not depict; they provide knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named; or they provide knowledge by analogy. Thus, definitions do not produce new conceptual knowledge; they only help identify what is being referred to, just as names do.

رسم
قول شارح
حجة
تصور
تصديق
عين
نوع

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Ḥadd: Names Over Definitions
Author argues that ḥadd provides weaker knowledge than names due to reliance on analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definitions (ḥadd) provide weaker knowledge than names because they rely on analogy instead of direct acquaintance.

Core Idea
Definitions give analogical knowledge; names give direct knowledge of the individual.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the logicians' confidence in ḥadd (essential definition). He argues that definitions do not give us direct knowledge of a thing but only knowledge by analogy, while a simple name can indicate the thing itself if we already know it. This critique has profound implications for how we use definitions in Islamic sciences such as fiqh and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What is the logicians' claim about ḥadd that Ibn Taymiyya is challenging? They assert that ḥadd provides true and certain knowledge of a thing's essence.
2
Follow the deconstruction via the intoxicant example
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of defining 'intoxicant' to show that ḥadd relies on prior knowledge of other concepts (like 'departure of intellect' and 'pleasure') and ultimately on analogy.
The definition uses a genus (departure of intellect) that includes sleep, insanity, etc., then adds a differentia (by something pleasurable).
3
Understand the epistemic distinction
He distinguishes between knowledge of the type (naw') and knowledge of the individual ('ayn). The definition only gives knowledge of the type, not the specific individual.
For example, one who never drank wine can only imagine its pleasure by analogy to bread and meat.
4
Note the qualification
Ibn Taymiyya does not reject ḥadd entirely; he says its benefit is of the same kind as names—a reminder (mudhakkira), not a formatter (muṣawwira); or knowledge by analogy.
He explicitly states: 'Our intent is not that the benefit of definitions is absolutely weaker.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن الكلام في تصوّره لمعنى المسكر كالكلام في تصوّره لمعنى الخمر. فإن لم يعرف عين المسكر وإلا لم يمكن تعريفه إلا بنظيره، فيقال: هو زوال العقل. وهذا جنس يشترك فيه النوم والجنون والإغماء والسكر. فلا بد أن يميَّزَ السكر فيقال زوال العقل بما يلتذ به. ثم اللذة لا بد أن يكون قد تصور جنسها بالأكل والشرب وغيرهما. وهذا يبيّن أن فائدة الحدود قد تكون أضعف من فائدة الأسماء، لأنها تفيد معرفة الشيء بنظيره، والاسم يكتفي به من عرفه بنفسه. وحقيقة الأمر أن الحد هو أن تصف المحدود بما تفصل به بينه وبين غيره. والصفات تفيد معرفة الموصوف خبراً، وليس المخبر كالمعاين، ولا من عرف المشهود عليه بعينه كمن عرفه بصفته وحليته. فمن عرف المسمى بعينه كان الاسم مُغنياً عن الحد كما تقدّم. ومن لم يعرفه بعينه لم يُفده الحدُّ ما يفيد الاسم لمن عرفه بعينه، إذ الاسم هناك يدلّ على العين التي عرفها بنفسها. والحدّ لمن لم يعرف العين إنما يفيد معرفة النوع، لا معرفة العين، كما يتصوّر لذة شرب الخمر من لم يشربها قياساً على اللذة بالخبز واللحم، ومعلوم فرق ما بين اللذتين. وليس مقصودنا أن فائدة الحدود أضعف مطلقاً، وإنما المقصود أنها من جنس فائدة الأسماء، وأنها مذكرة لا مصوّرة؛ أو معرفة بالتسمية، مميزة للمسمّى من غيره؛ أو معرفة بالقياس.
But the discussion about one's conceptualization of the meaning of 'intoxicant' is like the discussion about one's conceptualization of the meaning of 'wine'. For if he does not know the specific intoxicant, otherwise it cannot be defined except by its analogue, so it is said: It is the departure of intellect. And this is a genus in which share sleep, insanity, fainting, and intoxication. So it is necessary to differentiate intoxication, so it is said: The departure of intellect by something pleasurable. Then pleasure must have its genus previously conceptualized by eating, drinking, and others. And this shows that the benefit of definitions may be weaker than the benefit of names, because they give knowledge of a thing by its analogue, while a name suffices for the one who knows it by itself. And the reality is that a definition is that you describe the defined by what you distinguish it from others. And descriptions give knowledge of the described as a report, and the reporter is not like the eyewitness, nor is the one who knows the witnessed person by his very self like the one who knows him by his description and physical appearance. So whoever knows the named thing by its very self, the name suffices him from the definition as previously stated. And whoever does not know it by its very self, the definition does not give him what the name gives to the one who knows it by its very self, for the name there indicates the very self that he knew by itself. And the definition for the one who does not know the very self only gives knowledge of the type, not knowledge of the very self, just as the one who has not drunk wine conceptualizes the pleasure of drinking wine by analogy to the pleasure of bread and meat, and the difference between the two pleasures is known. And our intent is not that the benefit of definitions is absolutely weaker, but rather that it is of the same kind as the benefit of names, and that it is a reminder, not a formatter; or a knowledge by naming, distinguishing the named from others; or a knowledge by analogy.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the utility of ḥadd (definition) as understood by logicians. The author, Ibn Taymiyya, argues that ḥadd is epistemically weaker than simple names. He begins with an example: conceptualizing 'intoxicant' (musakkir) is similar to conceptualizing 'wine' (khamr). If one does not already know the specific intoxicant, it can only be defined by an analogue—e.g., 'departure of intellect provided by something pleasurable.' This definition uses a genus (departure of intellect) shared by sleep, insanity, fainting, and intoxication, then a differentia (by something pleasurable). But even then, the term 'pleasure' itself requires prior conceptualization via eating, drinking, etc. Thus, a definition only gives knowledge by analogy, whereas a name suffices if one already knows the thing directly. The author then states the reality of ḥadd: it describes the limited by what distinguishes it from others, but descriptions are reports, and a report is not like direct experience. For someone who knows the thing directly, the name suffices; for someone who does not, the definition gives only knowledge of the type (naw'), not the individual ('ayn). He illustrates with the pleasure of wine: one who never drank wine can only imagine it by analogy to bread and meat, but the two pleasures are different. Finally, he clarifies that he does not claim ḥadd is absolutely weaker, but that its benefit is of the same kind as names—a reminder (mudhakkira), not a formatter (musawwira); a knowledge by naming that distinguishes; or a knowledge by analogy.

الاسم
النوع

A) Because the name is shorter and easier to remember

C) Because ḥadd always contains errors
D) Because names are divinely revealed

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya does not reject definitions outright but argues that their epistemic value is limited to analogy and reminder; names can provide more direct knowledge for those already acquainted with the thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a ḥadd (definition) of a key Islamic term from a classical text of mantiq or uṣūl al-fiqh (e.g., 'qiyās' defined as 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared 'illah'). Write a brief critique following Ibn Taymiyya's argument: identify the analogical elements and explain why, according to him, this definition is merely a reminder rather than a source of direct knowledge. Then state whether the name 'qiyās' would be sufficient for someone already familiar with the concept.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Impossibility of Defining Primary Concepts
Ibn Taymiyya presents Ibn Sina's view on self-evident concepts and argues against defining them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem of circularity and infinite regress in attempting to define self-evident concepts like 'mawjud' and 'shay'.

Core Idea
Self-evident concepts cannot be defined; any attempted definition is either circular or leads to infinite regress.

Ibn Taymiyya, in his Radd al-Mantiqiyyin, cites Ibn Sina's own admission that some concepts are self-conceived. Building on this, he argues that any attempt to define such primary concepts is logically flawed. He critiques two definitions as examples, showing how they fall into circularity. This lesson lays out the general argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's claim about primary concepts
Find where Ibn Sina says that concepts like 'existence' and 'thing' are imprinted in the soul without needing prior definition.
2
Recognize the parallel between primary conceptions and primary assents
Note that just as there are self-evident premises (tasdiq), there are self-evident concepts (tasawwur).
3
Understand the function of definitions as reminders (tanbih)
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions of primary concepts can only remind, not inform, because the concepts are already known.
4
Grasp the consequence of infinite regress or circularity
If every conception needed a prior definition, it would go on forever or circle back to the same term.
5
Apply the test: check if a definition uses less-known terms
A real definition must use terms more known than the definiendum; if not, it fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته
اعتراف ابن سينا بأن من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته
وهكذا يقول حذاقهم في تحديد أمور كثيرة قد حدّها غيرهم. يقولون: لا يمكن تحديدها تحديد تعريف لماهياتها بل تحديد تنبيه وتمييز. كما قال ابن سينا في «الشفاء». قال :
فنقول : إن الموجود و الشيء و الضروري معانيها ترتسم في النفس ارتساماً أولياً - ليس ذلك الارتسام مما يحتاج أن يُجلب بأشياء هو أعرف منها . فإنه كما أنه في باب التصديق مبادىء أولية يقع التصديق بها لذاتها، ويكون التصديق لغيرها بسببها . وإذا لم يخطر بالبال أو لم يفهم اللفظ الدال عليها لم يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة ما يعرف بها . وإذا لم يكن التعريف الذي يحاول إخطارها بالبال، أو تفهيم ما يدل عليها من الألفاظ محاولاً لإفادة علم ما ليس في الغريزة، بل منبهاً على تفهيم ما يريده القائلُ أو يذهب إليه. وربما كان ذلك بأشياء هي في أنفسها أخفى من المراد تعريفه لكنها لعلة ما وعبارة ما صارت أعرف .
كذلك في التصورات أشياء هي مبادىء للتصوّر - هي متصوّرات لذاتها . وإذا أريد أن يُدَلَّ عليها لم يكن ذلك بالحقيقة تعريفاً لمجهول، بل تنبيهاً وإخطاراً بالبال باسم العلامة . وربما كانت في أنفسها أخفى منه لكنها لعلة ما وحال ما تكون أظهر دلالة. فإذا استعملت تلك العلامة نبهت النفس على إخطار ذلك المعنى بالبال من حيث أنه هو المراد لا غيره من غير أن تكون العلامة بالحقيقة معلمة إياه.
ولو كان كلُّ تصورٍ يحتاج إلى أن يسبقه تصور قبله لذهب الأمر إلى غير النهاية، أو لَدَار. وأولى الأشياء بأن تكون متصوّرةً لأنفُسها الأشياء العامة للأمور كلها، كالموجود والشيء والواحد وغيره. ولهذا ليس يمكن أن يبين شيء منها بيان لا دَوْرَ فيه ألبتة، أو ببيان و شيء أعرف منها .
وكذلك من حاول أن يقول فيها شيئاً وقع في اضطراب. كمن يقول: إن من حقيقة الموجود أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً . وهذا وإن كان ولا بد فمن أقسام الموجود والموجود أعرفُ من الفاعل والمنفَعِل وجُمْهُورُ الناس يتصوّرون حقيقة الموجود ولا يعرفون ألبتة أنه يجب أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً . وأنا إلى هذه الغاية لم يتضح لي ذلك إلا بقياس لا غير . فكيف يكون حال من يروم أن يعرف الشيء الظاهر بصفة له تحتاج إلى بيان حتى يُثبت وجودها له !
وكذلك قول من قال: إن الشيء هو الذي يصح عنه الخبر . فإن يصح أخفى من الشيء والخبرُ أخفى من الشيء فكيف يكون هذا تعريفاً للشيء! وإنما تُعرف الصحة ويُعرف الخبر بعد أن يستعمل في بيان كل واحد منهما أنه شيء أو أنه أمر أو أنه ما أو أنه الذي وجميع كالمرادفات لاسم الشيء . فكيف يصح أن يعرف الشيء تعريفاً حقيقياً بما لا يعرف إلا به !
ذلك
نعم ربما كان في ذلك وأمثاله تنبيه ما . وأما بالحقيقة فإنك إذا قلت إن الحد ربما ينبه تنبيهاً
The second station - the fourth aspect: among things there is what is conceived by itself. Ibn Sina's admission that among things there is what is conceived by itself. And thus their experts say in defining many things that others have defined. They say: It is not possible to define them with a definition that defines their quiddities, but rather a definition of reminder and distinction. As Ibn Sina said in 'al-Shifa'. He said: We say: 'Existence', 'Thing', and 'Necessary' — their meanings are imprinted in the soul with a primary imprint; that imprint does not need to be brought by things more known than it. For just as in the domain of assent there are primary principles that are assented to by themselves, and assent to other things is through them. And if the expression indicating them does not occur to the mind or is not understood, it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of what is known through them. And if the definition that attempts to bring them to mind or to make understood what indicates them from expressions is not attempting to convey knowledge of what is not in the innate disposition, but rather reminding of the understanding of what the speaker intends or holds. And perhaps that is by things that are in themselves more hidden than the intended object of definition, but due to some cause and some expression they become more known. Likewise in conceptions there are things that are principles for conception — they are conceived by themselves. And if one wants to indicate them, that is not in reality a definition of an unknown, but rather a reminder and bringing to mind by the name of a sign. And perhaps they are in themselves more hidden than it, but due to some cause and some state they are more apparent in indication. So when that sign is used, it alerts the soul to bring that meaning to mind in that it is the intended meaning not other, without the sign being in reality a teacher of it. And if every conception needed to be preceded by a conception before it, the matter would go to infinity or would circle. And the most worthy things to be conceived by themselves are the universal things for all matters, like 'Existence', 'Thing', 'One' and others. And therefore it is not possible to explain any of them with an explanation that has absolutely no circularity, or with an explanation and a thing more known than them. Likewise, whoever tries to say something about them falls into confusion. Like one who says: 'The reality of Existence is to be an agent or a patient.' And this, although it is necessary, is among the divisions of Existence, and Existence is more known than Agent and Patient, and the majority of people conceive the reality of Existence and do not know at all that it must be an agent or a patient. And up to this point it has not become clear to me except by syllogism, not otherwise. So how is the case of one who aims to define the manifest thing by a quality of it that needs explanation until its existence for it is proven! And likewise the saying of one who said: 'Thing is that about which a report is valid.' For 'is valid' is more hidden than 'Thing', and 'report' is more hidden than 'Thing', so how can this be a definition of 'Thing'! Rather, 'validity' is known and 'report' is known only after it is used in explaining each one of them that it is a 'thing' or that it is an 'affair' or that it is 'what' or that it is 'that which', and all are like synonyms for the name 'Thing'. So how is it valid to define 'Thing' with a real definition by what is only known through it! Yes, perhaps in that and its likes there is some reminder. But in reality, if you say that the definition perhaps reminds a reminder...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya (in his Radd al-Mantiqiyyin) of the logical doctrine regarding primary concepts and their definitions. The author begins by citing Ibn Sina's admission that some concepts are self-conceived (mutasawwar bi dhatihi). Ibn Sina, in al-Shifa', explains that terms like 'existence' (al-mawjud), 'thing' (al-shay), and 'necessary' (al-daruri) are primary: their meanings are imprinted in the soul without needing more known things. This is analogous to primary premises in the domain of assent (tasdiq). True definitions merely remind, not inform, of these self-evident concepts. Ibn Taymiyya then builds on this to critique attempted definitions of these primary concepts. First, he argues that if every conception required a prior conception, there would be either infinite regress or circularity. The most universal concepts (like existence and thing) are the most self-evident, so they cannot be defined without circularity. He then gives two concrete examples of faulty definitions. The first is defining 'existence' as 'being an agent or patient' — but existence is more known than its divisions, and the division itself requires proof. The second is defining 'thing' as 'that about which a report is valid' — here both 'valid' and 'report' are less known and themselves rely on 'thing' for their definitions, leading to circularity. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that such attempts may serve as reminders but are not real definitions. He laces his critique with sharp rhetorical questions, emphasizing the failure of the logicians' approach. This passage demonstrates Ibn Taymiyya's method: he uses the logicians' own authorities (Ibn Sina) against them, then shows the logical flaws in their definitions.

Key Terms
المتصور بذاته
الموجود
الشيء
التنبيه
الدور

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is it impossible to define primary concepts like 'mawjud' and 'shay'?
A) Because they are too complex.
B) Because they are self-evident and any definition would be circular or lead to infinite regress.
C) Because the logicians lack the proper methodology.
D) Because they are not real concepts.

Key Takeaway
Primary concepts cannot be defined; definitions of them can only serve as reminders.

Examine a typical definition of 'existence' from a Kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusiyya's Umm al-Barahin). Identify whether the definition uses terms that are less known than existence. If so, note the circularity.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Defining 'Mawjud' Through Its Divisions
Ibn Taymiyya critiques a specific definition of 'mawjud' as 'fa'il or munfa'il'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why defining a primary concept by one of its less-known divisions is a logical error.

Core Idea
You cannot define a self-evident concept using a less-known division that itself requires proof.

Ibn Taymiyya brings up a specific example: some define 'existence' as 'being an agent or patient'. He points out that existence is more known than agent or patient, and the division itself is not self-evident. This lesson dissects that critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being tested
The definition: 'al-mawjud is that which is either fa'il or munfa'il'. Locate this in the passage.
2
Recognize Ibn Taymiyya's criterion for definitions
A definition must use terms more known than the definiendum. Check if 'fa'il' and 'munfa'il' are more known than 'mawjud'.
3
Verify the relative familiarity
Ibn Taymiyya argues that 'mawjud' is more known to the majority; the classification into agent/patient itself requires a syllogistic proof.
4
Note the violation of definition rules
The definition uses less-known terms and relies on a non-self-evident division, so it fails to clarify.
5
Apply the lesson to other examples
Find a similar definition in Islamic sciences (e.g., defining 'knowledge' as 'necessary or acquired') and test it.
