⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته
اعتراف ابن سينا بأن من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته
وهكذا يقول حذاقهم في تحديد أمور كثيرة قد حدّها غيرهم. يقولون: لا يمكن تحديدها تحديد تعريف لماهياتها بل تحديد تنبيه وتمييز. كما قال ابن سينا في «الشفاء». قال :
فنقول : إن الموجود و الشيء و الضروري معانيها ترتسم في النفس ارتساماً أولياً - ليس ذلك الارتسام مما يحتاج أن يُجلب بأشياء هو أعرف منها . فإنه كما أنه في باب التصديق مبادىء أولية يقع التصديق بها لذاتها، ويكون التصديق لغيرها بسببها . وإذا لم يخطر بالبال أو لم يفهم اللفظ الدال عليها لم يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة ما يعرف بها . وإذا لم يكن التعريف الذي يحاول إخطارها بالبال، أو تفهيم ما يدل عليها من الألفاظ محاولاً لإفادة علم ما ليس في الغريزة، بل منبهاً على تفهيم ما يريده القائلُ أو يذهب إليه. وربما كان ذلك بأشياء هي في أنفسها أخفى من المراد تعريفه لكنها لعلة ما وعبارة ما صارت أعرف .
كذلك في التصورات أشياء هي مبادىء للتصوّر - هي متصوّرات لذاتها . وإذا أريد أن يُدَلَّ عليها لم يكن ذلك بالحقيقة تعريفاً لمجهول، بل تنبيهاً وإخطاراً بالبال باسم العلامة . وربما كانت في أنفسها أخفى منه لكنها لعلة ما وحال ما تكون أظهر دلالة. فإذا استعملت تلك العلامة نبهت النفس على إخطار ذلك المعنى بالبال من حيث أنه هو المراد لا غيره من غير أن تكون العلامة بالحقيقة معلمة إياه.
ولو كان كلُّ تصورٍ يحتاج إلى أن يسبقه تصور قبله لذهب الأمر إلى غير النهاية، أو لَدَار. وأولى الأشياء بأن تكون متصوّرةً لأنفُسها الأشياء العامة للأمور كلها، كالموجود والشيء والواحد وغيره. ولهذا ليس يمكن أن يبين شيء منها بيان لا دَوْرَ فيه ألبتة، أو ببيان و شيء أعرف منها .
وكذلك من حاول أن يقول فيها شيئاً وقع في اضطراب. كمن يقول: إن من حقيقة الموجود أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً . وهذا وإن كان ولا بد فمن أقسام الموجود والموجود أعرفُ من الفاعل والمنفَعِل وجُمْهُورُ الناس يتصوّرون حقيقة الموجود ولا يعرفون ألبتة أنه يجب أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً . وأنا إلى هذه الغاية لم يتضح لي ذلك إلا بقياس لا غير . فكيف يكون حال من يروم أن يعرف الشيء الظاهر بصفة له تحتاج إلى بيان حتى يُثبت وجودها له !
وكذلك قول من قال: إن الشيء هو الذي يصح عنه الخبر . فإن يصح أخفى من الشيء والخبرُ أخفى من الشيء فكيف يكون هذا تعريفاً للشيء! وإنما تُعرف الصحة ويُعرف الخبر بعد أن يستعمل في بيان كل واحد منهما أنه شيء أو أنه أمر أو أنه ما أو أنه الذي وجميع كالمرادفات لاسم الشيء . فكيف يصح أن يعرف الشيء تعريفاً حقيقياً بما لا يعرف إلا به !
ذلك
نعم ربما كان في ذلك وأمثاله تنبيه ما . وأما بالحقيقة فإنك إذا قلت إن الحد ربما ينبه تنبيهاً
The second station - the fourth aspect: among things there is what is conceived by itself. Ibn Sina's admission that among things there is what is conceived by itself. And thus their experts say in defining many things that others have defined. They say: It is not possible to define them with a definition that defines their quiddities, but rather a definition of reminder and distinction. As Ibn Sina said in 'al-Shifa'. He said: We say: 'Existence', 'Thing', and 'Necessary' — their meanings are imprinted in the soul with a primary imprint; that imprint does not need to be brought by things more known than it. For just as in the domain of assent there are primary principles that are assented to by themselves, and assent to other things is through them. And if the expression indicating them does not occur to the mind or is not understood, it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of what is known through them. And if the definition that attempts to bring them to mind or to make understood what indicates them from expressions is not attempting to convey knowledge of what is not in the innate disposition, but rather reminding of the understanding of what the speaker intends or holds. And perhaps that is by things that are in themselves more hidden than the intended object of definition, but due to some cause and some expression they become more known. Likewise in conceptions there are things that are principles for conception — they are conceived by themselves. And if one wants to indicate them, that is not in reality a definition of an unknown, but rather a reminder and bringing to mind by the name of a sign. And perhaps they are in themselves more hidden than it, but due to some cause and some state they are more apparent in indication. So when that sign is used, it alerts the soul to bring that meaning to mind in that it is the intended meaning not other, without the sign being in reality a teacher of it. And if every conception needed to be preceded by a conception before it, the matter would go to infinity or would circle. And the most worthy things to be conceived by themselves are the universal things for all matters, like 'Existence', 'Thing', 'One' and others. And therefore it is not possible to explain any of them with an explanation that has absolutely no circularity, or with an explanation and a thing more known than them. Likewise, whoever tries to say something about them falls into confusion. Like one who says: 'The reality of Existence is to be an agent or a patient.' And this, although it is necessary, is among the divisions of Existence, and Existence is more known than Agent and Patient, and the majority of people conceive the reality of Existence and do not know at all that it must be an agent or a patient. And up to this point it has not become clear to me except by syllogism, not otherwise. So how is the case of one who aims to define the manifest thing by a quality of it that needs explanation until its existence for it is proven! And likewise the saying of one who said: 'Thing is that about which a report is valid.' For 'is valid' is more hidden than 'Thing', and 'report' is more hidden than 'Thing', so how can this be a definition of 'Thing'! Rather, 'validity' is known and 'report' is known only after it is used in explaining each one of them that it is a 'thing' or that it is an 'affair' or that it is 'what' or that it is 'that which', and all are like synonyms for the name 'Thing'. So how is it valid to define 'Thing' with a real definition by what is only known through it! Yes, perhaps in that and its likes there is some reminder. But in reality, if you say that the definition perhaps reminds a reminder...

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Defining 'Shay' via 'Valid Report'
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the definition of 'shay' as 'that about which a report is valid'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect circularity when a definition uses terms that themselves depend on the definiendum.

Core Idea
Defining 'thing' by 'valid report' is circular because 'report' and 'valid' are only understood in terms of 'thing'.

Ibn Taymiyya targets a common logical definition: 'thing is that about which a report is valid'. He points out that 'report' and 'validity' are themselves less known and rely on 'thing' for their own definitions. This lesson explores that circularity.

Guided Path
1
State the definition under attack
The definition: 'al-shay = ma yasihh 'anhu al-khabar'. Locate this in the passage.
2
Analyze the terms used
'Khabar' (report) and 'sihha' (validity) are abstract and less known than 'shay'. How are they defined? They are defined by 'shay' itself.
3
Identify the circularity
To know 'shay', you need to know 'khabar'; but to know 'khabar', you need to know 'shay'. This is a vicious circle.
4
Consider the implication
The definition fails as a real definition because it presupposes the definiendum. At best, it can serve as a reminder (tanbih).
5
Apply the test to other definitions
Find a definition in Islamic sciences where the definiens uses terms that are defined by the definiendum.

D) The definition includes an unnecessary condition.

Key Takeaway
A definition must not presuppose the definiendum; otherwise, it is circular and fails to inform.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition of 'hukm' from a usul text and check if any term in the definition is itself defined by 'hukm'.

The Impossibility of Defining 'al-Mawjud' and 'al-Shay'
Author critiques attempts to define 'al-mawjud' and 'al-shay'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circularity in attempts to define the most universal concepts and explain why they must be self-evident.

Core Idea
The most universal concepts cannot be defined; any attempt leads to circularity or infinite regress.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya deconstructs the Aristotelian project of defining basic concepts like 'existent' (al-mawjud) and 'thing' (al-shay'). He argues that such concepts are self-evident and that any attempted definition either relies on terms more obscure than the definiendum or ends in circularity. This critique helps us recognize the limits of definition and the necessity of accepting certain primitive notions in any science.

Guided Path
1
Understand the infinite regress argument
The author begins by stating that if every conception required a prior conception, we would have infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, some concepts must be conceived by themselves (self-evident).
2
Identify the self-evident concepts
The author lists 'al-mawjud', 'al-shay', and 'al-wahid' (the one) as examples of the most universal concepts that are known directly.
These are 'first principles' of thought.
3
Critique the definition of 'existent' as 'agent or patient'
Ibn Taymiyya shows that this is a division of 'existent', not a definition. 'Existent' is more known than 'agent' or 'patient', and most people understand 'existent' without knowing those categories.
The author even admits he himself only grasped that division through reasoning.
4
Critique the definition of 'thing' as 'that which is valid to be informed about'
He points out that 'valid' and 'information' are less known than 'thing', and the definition circularly uses synonyms (e.g., 'what', 'which' are equivalent to 'thing').
The definition effectively says 'thing is the thing that is valid to be informed about'.
5
Recognize the intended conclusion
The author concludes that such attempts at best provide vague indication, but true definition is impossible. The meanings of 'existent' and 'thing' are directly conceived in the soul.
This is a foundational critique against overly ambitious definitional projects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون أظهر دلالة. فإذا استعملت تلك العلامة نبهت النفس على إخطار ذلك المعنى بالبال من حيث أنه هو المراد لا غيره من غير أن تكون العلامة بالحقيقة معلمة إياه. ولو كان كل تصور يحتاج إلى أن يسبقه تصور قبله لذهب الأمر إلى غير النهاية، أو لدار. وأولى الأشياء بأن تكون متصورة لأنفسها الأشياء العامة للأمور كلها، كـ«الموجود» و«الشيء» و«الواحد» وغيره. ولهذا ليس يمكن أن يبين شيء منها بيان لا دور فيه ألبتة، أو ببيان وشيء أعرف منها. وكذلك من حاول أن يقول فيها شيئاً وقع في اضطراب. كمن يقول: «إن من حقيقة (الموجود) أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً». وهذا، وإن كان ولا بد، فمن أقسام «الموجود»، و «الموجود» أعرف من «الفاعل» و«المنفعل»، وجمهور الناس يتصورون حقيقة الموجود ولا يعرفون ألبتة أنه يجب أن يكون فاعلاً أو منفعلاً. وأنا إلى هذه الغاية لم يتضح لي ذلك إلا بقياس، لا غير. فكيف يكون حال من يروم أن يعرف الشيء الظاهر بصفة له تحتاج إلى بيان حتى يثبت وجودها له! وكذلك قول من قال: «إن الشيء هو الذي يصح عنه الخبر». فإن «يصح» أخفى من «الشيء»، و«الخبر» أخفى من «الشيء»، فكيف يكون هذا تعريفاً لـ «الشيء»! وإنما تعرف «الصحة» ويعرف «الخبر» بعد أن يستعمل في بيان كل واحد منهما أنه «شيء»، أو أنه «أمر»، أو أنه «ما»، أو أنه «الذي»، وجميع كالمرادفات لاسم «الشيء». فكيف يصح أن يعرف «الشيء» تعريفاً حقيقياً بما لا يعرف إلا به! نعم ربما كان في ذلك وأمثاله تنبيه ما. وأما بالحقيقة، فإنك إذا قلت: «إن الشيء هو ما يصح أن يخبر عنه» تكون كأنك قلت: إن «الشيء» هو «الشيء الذي يصح عنه الخبر»، لأن معنى «ما» و«الذي» و«الشيء» معنى واحد، فتكون قد أخذت «الشيء» في حد «الشيء». على أننا لا ننكر أن يقع بهذا وما أشبهه - مع فساد مأخذه - تنبيه بوجه ما على «الشيء». ونقول: إن معنى «الموجود» ومعنى «الشيء» متصوران في الأنفس، وهما معنيان. و«الموجود» و «المثبت» و «المحصل» أسماء مترادفة على معنى واحد. ولا شك أن معناها قد حصل في نفس من يقرأ هذا الكتاب.
They are most indicative. So if that sign is used, it alerts the soul to bring that meaning to mind insofar as it is the intended meaning and no other, without the sign actually being a true indicator of it. And if every conception required a preceding conception, then the matter would go on to infinity or be circular. The things most deserving of being conceived by themselves are the universal things of all matters, such as 'the existent', 'the thing', 'the one', and others. Hence, it is not possible to explain any of them with an explanation that is entirely free of circularity, or with an explanation and something more knowable than them. Likewise, whoever attempts to say something about them falls into confusion. Like one who says: 'It is part of the reality of the existent that it is an agent or a patient.' This, although necessary, is among the divisions of 'the existent', and 'the existent' is more known than 'the agent' and 'the patient', and the common people conceive the reality of the existent without knowing at all that it must be an agent or patient. And as for me, up to this point, that has not become clear to me except by analogy, nothing else. So how is the case of one who intends to define the evident thing by a property that needs proof to establish its existence for it! Similarly, the statement of one who said: 'The thing is that which is valid to be informed about.' For 'valid' is more obscure than 'thing', and 'information' is more obscure than 'thing', so how can this be a definition of 'thing'! Rather, 'validity' and 'information' are only known after using in explaining each of them that it is a 'thing', or an 'affair', or a 'what', or a 'which', all of which are synonyms of the name 'thing'. So how can it be valid to define 'thing' by a true definition with something that is only known through it! Yes, perhaps in that and its likes there is some indication. But in reality, if you say: 'The thing is that which is valid to be informed about,' it is as if you said: 'The thing is the thing that is valid to be informed about,' because the meaning of 'what', 'which', and 'thing' is one meaning, so you have taken 'thing' in the definition of 'thing'. However, we do not deny that by this and the like - despite the corruption of its approach - there is some indication of 'the thing'. And we say: The meaning of 'the existent' and the meaning of 'the thing' are conceived in the souls, and they are two meanings. And 'the existent', 'the established', and 'the realized' are synonymous names for one meaning. There is no doubt that their meaning has been obtained in the soul of whoever reads this book.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of attempts to define the most basic concepts — specifically 'al-mawjud' (the existent) and 'al-shay' (the thing). The author (Ibn Taymiyya) argues that these concepts are self-evident and cannot be defined without circularity or using something more obscure. He begins by stating that if every conception required a prior conception, there would be an infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, the most universal notions must be conceived by themselves. Examples include 'existent', 'thing', 'one'. No definition of them can avoid circularity. He then attacks specific attempts: defining 'existent' as 'agent or patient' — this is a division, not a definition, and 'existent' is more known than 'agent' and 'patient'. Most people understand 'existent' without knowing those categories. The author even admits that for him, that classification became clear only through reasoning, not immediate intuition. Next, he critiques the definition of 'thing' as 'that which is valid to be informed about' — pointing out that 'valid' and 'information' are less known than 'thing', and that to know them you already need the concept of 'thing'. The definition is circular because it uses synonyms. He concludes by acknowledging that such pseudo-definitions might provide some vague indication, but they are not true definitions. The correct view is that these meanings are simply and directly understood by anyone reading this book.

Key Terms
الموجود (al-mawjud): The existent; one of the most universal concepts that is self-evident and cannot be defined without circularity. [Note: Standard in Islamic theology and philosophy; Ibn Taymiyya uses it to argue against Aristotelian definitions.]
الشيء (al-shay'): The thing; another self-evident universal concept, often equated with 'al-mawjud' in meaning. [Note: In later logic, 'shay'' is sometimes distinguished from 'mawjud'; here they are treated as synonyms.]
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition; explaining a term by itself or by something that presupposes it. [Note: Stable term in logic and usul al-fiqh as a fallacy.]

D) Because the definition uses an Aristotelian category.

Key Takeaway
The most universal concepts like 'existent' and 'thing' are self-evident and cannot be defined without circularity; any attempt to define them relies on concepts that are themselves dependent on the original term.

The Impossibility of Defining 'The One' and 'The Many'
Author shows that defining 'the one' and 'the many' is impossible because each inevitably references the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions of fundamental concepts and construct a critique showing why they must be self-evident.

Core Idea
Defining 'the one' and 'the many' is circular because each already contains the other.

The logicians attempted to define 'al-wahid' and 'al-kathir' as basic concepts, but all definitions fail because they rely on the very term being defined. Ibn Sina's critique, presented here, reveals a deep problem: the most fundamental realities cannot be captured by formal definitions. In this lesson, we will dissect these circular definitions and understand why some concepts must be accepted as self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of 'al-wahid' and 'al-kathir'
Locate the definitions the author considers: 'the one is that which is not divisible' and 'the many is the aggregate of units' or 'that which is counted by the one'.
2
Extract the circularity in each definition
For 'the one is indivisible', note that 'division' implies parts, thus multiplicity. For 'the many is the aggregate of units', 'unit' is synonymous with 'one', and 'aggregate' itself implies the many. The definition uses what it tries to define.
Circularity is vicious because it provides no independent understanding.
3
Generalize the pattern
Recognize that any definition of these primitives will inevitably appeal to the other. This shows that 'al-wahid' and 'al-kathir' are mutually dependent and cannot be defined in a non-circular way.
The same applies to other pairs like 'cause and effect' or 'substance and accident'.
4
Conclude that these concepts are self-evident
Since formal definition fails, these concepts must be grasped directly without definition. This is a key insight in Islamic logic and kalam: primary concepts are known by necessity (darura) and need no definition.
This critique grounds the distinction between genuine definitions and mere descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قال في حد «الواحد» و«الكثير». قال: فصل في تحقيق «الواحد» و«الكثير»، وإبانة أن «العدد» عرض، والذي يصعب صعوبة حد «الواحد» علينا تحقيقه ماهية «الواحد». وذلك أنا إذا قلنا: «إن الواحد: الذي لا ينقسم» فقد قلنا إن الواحد: الذي لا يتكثر» ضرورة، فأخذنا في بيان الواحد «الكثرة». وأما الكثرة فمن الضرورة أن تحد بـ«الواحد» لأن «الواحد» مبدأ الكثرة، ومنه وجودها وماهيتها. فأي حد حددنا به «الكثرة» استعملنا فيه «الواحد» بالضرورة. فمن ذلك ما حد «الكثرة» نقول: إن الكثرة هو المجتمع من وحدات؛ فقد أخذنا «الوحدة» في حد الكثرة. ثم عملنا شيئاً آخر، وهو أنا أخذنا «المجتمع» في حدها، و«المجتمع» يشبه أن يكون هو الكثرة نفسها. وإذا قلنا: من الوحدات أو الوحدان أو الآحاد فقد أوردنا بدل لفظ «الجمع» هذا اللفظ، ولا يفهم معناه ولا يعرف إلا بـ«الكثرة». وإذا قلنا: «إن الكثرة هي التي تعد بالواحد» فنكون قد أخذنا
And likewise he said regarding the definition of 'the one' and 'the many'. He said: A chapter on verifying 'the one' and 'the many', and clarifying that 'number' is an accident, and what is difficult for us to verify is the quiddity of 'the one'. That is because if we say: 'The one is that which is not divisible,' then we have necessarily said: 'The one is that which does not become many,' thus we have taken 'the many' in the explanation of 'the one'. As for 'the many', it is necessarily defined by 'the one' because 'the one' is the principle of 'the many', and from it comes its existence and quiddity. So whichever definition we define 'the many' with, we necessarily use 'the one' in it. For example, we define 'the many' by saying: 'The many is the aggregate of units'; so we have taken 'the unit' in the definition of 'the many'. Then we do something else, which is that we take 'the aggregate' in its definition, and 'the aggregate' resembles 'the many' itself. And if we say: 'from units' or 'from unities' or 'from ones', we have substituted the word 'plural' with this word, and its meaning is not understood or known except through 'the many'. And if we say: 'The many is that which is counted by the one,' then we would have taken...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage examines the problem of defining two fundamental concepts: 'al-wahid' (the one) and 'al-kathir' (the many). The author, likely quoting or paraphrasing Ibn Sina, shows that any attempt to define one inevitably involves the other, creating a vicious circle. He starts by considering the definition of 'the one' as 'that which is not divisible'. This definition implicitly uses the concept of division into parts, which implies multiplicity, so 'the many' is already employed. Conversely, defining 'the many' requires 'the one' because the many is composed of ones. He illustrates with the definition 'the many is the aggregate of units' — here 'unit' (wahda) is synonymous with 'one', so the definition is circular. Next, the term 'aggregate' itself resembles the many. If we replace 'units' with 'ones' or 'unities', we are still using a synonym for 'plural' which is understood only through the concept of 'the many'. Finally, the definition 'the many is that which is counted by the one' also takes 'the one' in the definition. The passage ends mid-sentence but clearly establishes that no non-circular definition is possible. This reinforces the earlier point that the most basic concepts are self-evident and cannot be defined properly.

العدد

Circularity in Defining Primitive Concepts: One and Many
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the impossibility of defining 'one' and 'many' without circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circularity in definitions of 'one' and 'many' and explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that they are primitive notions.

Core Idea
Defining 'one' requires 'many', and defining 'many' requires 'one'—circularity shows these concepts are primitive, not definable.

Ibn Taymiyya tackles a foundational problem: can the most basic concepts like 'one' and 'many' be defined without circular reasoning? He demonstrates that the logicians' definitions inevitably presuppose each other, exposing a vicious circle. His conclusion is that such concepts are not definable but are intuitively understood (badihi). This lesson helps you recognize circular definitions in Islamic sciences and appreciate the limits of definitional projects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under critique
In the passage, Ibn Taymiyya examines the logicians' definitions: 'the one is that which is not divided', and 'the many is a collection of units'. Write them down clearly.
2
Trace the circularity in defining 'one'
Notice that the definition of 'one' uses 'division' (inna 'l-wāḥida l-ladhi lā yanqasimu) which implies multiplicity (kathra). So defining 'one' invokes 'many'.
3
Trace the circularity in defining 'many'
The definition of 'many' uses 'units' (wāḥidāt) which are a plurality of 'ones'. So defining 'many' invokes 'one'. Each definition requires the other term.
4
Recognize the tautology in defining 'number'
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the definition of 'number' as 'a multiplicity composed of units' is tautological because 'multiplicity' (kathra) is identical to 'number' (ʿadad). The definition adds no new information.
5
Understand the alternative: primitive concepts
Ibn Taymiyya suggests that 'one' and 'many' are intuitive (badīhī) and do not require definition. They are known directly, not through composition from other concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : صعوبة حد «الواحد» و«الكثير». قال: فصل في تحقيق «الواحد» و «الكثير»، وإبانة أن «العدد» عَرَض، والذي يصعب صعوبة حد «الواحد» علينا تحقيقه ماهيّة الواحد. وذلك أنا إذا قلنا : «إن الواحد: (الذي لا ينقسم)» فقد قلنا إن الواحد: (الذي لا يتكثر)» ضرورة، فأخذنا في بيان الواحد «الكثرة». وأما الكثرة فمن الضرورة أن تُحدَّ بـ «الواحد» لأن «الواحد» مبدأ الكثرة، ومنه وجودها وماهيّتُها. أي حدّ حدّدنا به «الكثرة استعملنا فيه الواحد بالضرورة. فمن ذلك ما حد «الكثرة» نقول: إن (الكثرة) هو (المجتَمَع من وحدات)؛ فقد أخذنا «الوَحْدة» في حد «الكثرة». ثم عملنا شيئاً آخر، وهو أنا أخذنا «المجتمع» في حدّها، و«المجتمع» يشبه أن يكون هو الكثرة نفسها وإذا قلنا : «من الوحدات» أو «الوُحْدان» أو «الآحاد» فقد أوردنا بدل لفظ «الجمع» هذا اللفظ، ولا يفهم معناه ولا يعرف إلا بـ «الكثرة). وإذا قلنا : ((إن الكثرة) هي التي تعدّ بالواحد فنكون قد أخذنا في حد «الكثرة» «الوحدة»، ونكون أيضاً أخذنا في حدّها «العدد» و«التقدير»، وذلك إنما يفهم بـ «الكثرة» أيضاً. فما أغنى علينا أن نقول في هذا الباب شيئاً يعتد به، لكنه يُشبه أن تكون الكثرة أيضاً أعرف عند تخيلنا، ويشبه أن تكون «الوحدة» و«الكثرة» من الأمور التي نتصوّرها بُدّيّاً. فذكر «الكثرة» نتخيلها أولاً، و«الوحدة» نعقلها من غير مبدإ لتصورها. ثم إن كان ولا بد، فخيالي. ثم تعريفنا «الكثرة» بـ «الوحدة» تعريفاً عقلياً، وهنالك نأخذ «الوحدة» متصوّرة بذاتها. ومن أوائل التصوّر يكون تعريفنا «الوحدة» بـ «الكثرة» تنبيهاً يستعمل فيه المذهب الخيالي النومي إلى معقول عندنا، لا يتصور حاضراً في الذهن. فإذا قالوا : «إن (الوحدة) هي ( الشيء الذي ليست فيه كثرة)» دلُّوا على أن المراد بهذه اللفظة الشيء المعقول عندنا بُدّيّاً الذي يقابل هذا الآخر، وليس هو؛ فينبه عليه بسَلْب هذا عنه. والعجب ممن يحدّد «العدد» ويقول: «إن (العدد) كثرة مؤلفة من وحدة أو آحاد». والكثرة نفس «العدد»، ليس كالجنس لـ «العدد»، وحقيقةُ «الكثرة» أنها مؤلَّفة من وحدات. فقولهم : «إن (الكثرة) (مؤلفة من وحدات)» هو كقولهم : «إن العدد كثرة مؤلفة من وحدات». فلم يزد هذا تعريفا. فاعلم ذلك.
The second station - the fourth aspect: The difficulty of defining 'the one' and 'the many'. He said: A section on verifying 'the one' and 'the many', and clarifying that 'number' is an accident, and what makes it difficult to define 'the one' is verifying for us the essence of 'the one'. That is because if we say: 'The one is that which is not divided', then we have said necessarily: 'The one is that which does not become many', so we brought 'multiplicity' into the explanation of 'the one'. As for 'multiplicity', it is necessary to define it by 'the one' because 'the one' is the principle of 'multiplicity', and from it comes its existence and essence. So any definition we give of 'multiplicity' we necessarily use 'the one' in it. For example, when we define 'multiplicity' by saying: 'Multiplicity is a collection of units', we have taken 'unity' in the definition of 'multiplicity'. Then we do something else: we take 'collection' in its definition, and 'collection' seems to be multiplicity itself. And when we say 'of units' or 'unities' or 'ones', we have replaced the word 'plurality' with this word, and its meaning is not understood or known except by 'multiplicity'. And if we say: 'Multiplicity is what is counted by the one', then we have taken 'unity' in the definition of 'multiplicity', and we have also taken 'number' and 'estimation' in its definition, which are only understood by 'multiplicity' as well. So it is very far for us to say anything reliable on this topic, but it seems that 'multiplicity' is also more known in our imagination, and it seems that 'unity' and 'multiplicity' are among things that we conceive intuitively. So when we mention 'multiplicity' we imagine it first, and 'unity' we understand it without a principle for its conception. Then if it is necessary, it is imaginary. Then our definition of 'multiplicity' by 'unity' is a rational definition, and there we take 'unity' as conceived by itself. And from the first principles of conception is our definition of 'unity' by 'multiplicity' as a notification that uses the imaginary dreamlike method to reach something intelligible to us, not conceived as present in the mind. So if they say: 'Unity is the thing that has no multiplicity in it', they indicate that what is meant by this word is the thing that is intuitively intelligible to us that opposes this other, and it is not it; so they draw attention to it by negating this from it. And it is astonishing that someone defines 'number' and says: 'Number is a multiplicity composed of unity or units.' But 'multiplicity' is the same as 'number', not like a genus to 'number', and the reality of 'multiplicity' is that it is composed of units. So their saying: 'Multiplicity is composed of units' is like their saying: 'Number is a multiplicity composed of units.' So this does not add a definition. Know this.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' attempts to define 'one' and 'many'. He demonstrates that any definition of 'one' inevitably invokes 'many' (e.g., 'that which is not divided' implies division, which is multiplicity), and any definition of 'many' invokes 'one' (e.g., 'a collection of units' uses 'units', i.e., 'ones'). This circularity shows that these concepts are not properly definable. He then suggests that 'one' and 'many' may be primitive notions that we conceive intuitively, without need for definition. He further criticizes the definition of 'number' as 'a plurality composed of units', arguing that 'plurality' is identical to 'number', so the definition is tautological and adds no clarity. The passage follows the order: first, the problem of defining 'one'; second, the problem of defining 'many'; third, the suggestion that they are intuitive; fourth, the critique of the definition of number. Ibn Taymiyya's method is to expose logical circularity and to argue that certain fundamental concepts are not definable in a non-circular way.

Key Terms
الواحد
الكثير
العدد
الكثرة
الوحدة

Why does Ibn Taymiyya criticize the definition of 'number' as 'a multiplicity composed of units'?
A) Because 'multiplicity' is not the same as 'number'
B) Because the definition uses the word 'units' which is unclear
C) Because 'multiplicity' is identical to 'number', making the definition tautological
D) Because the definition fails to mention that number is an accident

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the concepts of 'one' and 'many' are not definable without circularity, suggesting they are primitive and intuitively known.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definitions of Multiplicity and Number
Ibn Taymiyya refutes definitions of quantity and number, showing circularity and that they are mere reminders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions in Islamic logical texts and distinguish genuine definitions from mere reminders (tanbih).

Core Idea
Defining multiplicity by unities is circular; definitions must reveal essences, not just remind.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks Avicenna's definitions of multiplicity (kathra) and number (ʿadad), showing they are circular because the definiens depends on the definiendum. He then generalizes that such definitions are merely reminders (tanbīhāt) for self-evident concepts. This critique is a powerful tool for evaluating whether any definition in Islamic logic or jurisprudence actually captures an essence or simply points to something already known.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under attack
Find the definitions of multiplicity (as 'composed of unities') and number (as 'discrete quantity having order') in the Arabic text. Note that these are standard Avicennan definitions.
The passage opens with 'they say: multiplicity is nothing but a name for what is composed of unities.'
2
Understand the circularity argument for multiplicity
Ibn Taymiyya argues that 'unities' themselves are only understood in relation to multiplicity, so defining multiplicity by unities is circular. Recognize that the definiens presupposes the definiendum.
He makes a similar point about 'parts' and 'division' requiring multiplicity.
3
Analyze the circularity in the definition of number
The definition of number includes 'order', but order cannot be understood without first understanding number. Similarly, 'equality' is an accident of quantity, but quantity is more known than equality. Trace this reasoning in the text.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'you cannot understand order except after understanding number.'
4
Recognize the concept of 'tanbih' (reminder)
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that these are not true definitions but mere reminders (tanbīhāt). They only point to self-evident concepts. Understand that for him, genuine definitions must reveal the essence, not just label what is already known.
He likens them to 'reminders by examples and synonymous names.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : المذكور في هذه الأمور يصح في سائر الأمور
وحدات ( ) كقولهم إن الكثرة ) ( كثرة ، فإن الكثرة) ليس إلا اسماً لـ «المؤلف من الوحدات» .
فإن قال قائل : إن الكثرة) قد تؤلف من أشياء غير الوحدات) مثل «الناس»، والدواب»، فنقول : إنه كما أن هذه الأشياء ليست «كثرات»، بل أشياء موضوعة لـ «الكثرة» ، وكما أن تلك الأشياء هي وحدان» لا «واحدات»، وكذلك هي كثيرة لا كثرة». والذين يحسبون أنهم إذا قالوا إن (العدد) (كمّية منفصلة ذات ترتيب فقد تخلصوا من هذا، فما تخلصوا .
فإن الكمية» تُخوج تصوُّرُها للنفس إلى أن تعرف بـ «الجزء» أو «القسمة» أو المساواة». أما «الجزء» و«القسمة) فإنما يمكن تصوّرهما بـ «الكثرة» . وأما المساواة)) فإن الكمية)) أعرفُ منها عند العقل الصريح، لأن «المساواة» من الأعراض الخاصة بـ «الكمية) التي يجب أن توخَذ في حدها «الكمية)، فيقال «إن (اتحاد في الكمية» و«الترتيب الذي أخذ في حدّ «العدد» أيضاً ما لا يفهم إلا بعد فهم (العدد» .
فيجب أن يعلم أن هذه كلها تنبيهات مثل التنبيه بالأمثلة والأسماء المترادفة، هي تنبيهات فحسب فإن هذه المعاني متصوّرة كلها أو بعضها لذواتها، وإنما يُدلُّ عليها بهذه الأشياء لينبه عليها ويميّز فقط .
قلت : فهذا الكلام الذي ذكره ابن سينا هنا قد تلقوه عنه بالقبول، كما يذكر مثل ذلك أبو حامد والرازي ، والسُّهروردي وغيرهم.
Second station - Fourth aspect: What is mentioned about these matters applies to all other matters.
Unities ( ) as they say: 'multiplicity is nothing but a name for what is composed of unities.'
If someone says: 'Multiplicity may be composed of things other than unities, like people and animals,' we say: Just as these things are not 'unities' but things subject to 'unity,' and just as those things are 'unities' not 'unities' (wāḥidāt), and likewise they are 'many' not 'multiplicity.' And those who think that when they say 'Number is a discrete quantity having order' they have escaped this, have not escaped.
For the conception of 'quantity' brings the soul to know it by 'part' or 'division' or 'equality.' As for 'part' and 'division,' they can only be conceived through 'multiplicity.' And as for 'equality,' 'quantity' is more known than it according to sound reason, because 'equality' is among the accidents specific to 'quantity' that must be taken in its definition of 'quantity,' so it is said 'it is a union in quantity.' And the 'order' taken in the definition of 'number' likewise cannot be understood except after understanding 'number.'
So it must be known that all these are only reminders, like reminder by examples and synonymous names; they are only reminders, because these concepts are all or some of them self-evident, and they are only indicated by these things to remind and distinguish them.
I say: This speech that Ibn Sina mentioned here has been accepted by them, as Abu Hamid, al-Razi, al-Suhrawardi and others mention similar things.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of Avicenna's definitions of multiplicity (kathra) and number (ʿadad). Ibn Taymiyya argues that defining multiplicity as 'composed of unities' is circular because unities themselves are understood only in relation to multiplicity. Similarly, the definition of number as 'discrete quantity having order' fails because order presupposes number. He then generalizes that such 'definitions' are merely reminders (tanbīhāt) for self-evident concepts, not genuine definitions that reveal essences. He notes that even Avicenna's followers have accepted this problematic approach.

B) It is circular because unities are understood through multiplicity.
C) It only applies to people and animals.
D) It is actually a perfect definition.

Key Takeaway
Genuine definitions must reveal essences; if a definition presupposes the concept it defines, it is merely a reminder.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Limits of Definition
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies the epistemological limits of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya claims that definitions for hidden matters only provide distinction, not essence.

Core Idea
Definitions distinguish hidden matters, they do not reveal their true essence.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definitions give complete knowledge. He argues that for hidden matters, definitions only provide distinction and resemblance-based knowledge, not the essence itself. This is a foundational critique in his larger project.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about definitions
Look for where Ibn Taymiyya states what definitions provide and what they fail to provide. He says: 'mere definitions do not provide knowledge of their true essence, but only serve to distinguish them and provide a kind of resemblance-based definition.'
This is the core of his critique.
2
Contrast with common matters
Note how he differentiates between matters clear to all and hidden matters. Common matters are known without definition; hidden matters require definition but even then it is insufficient.
3
Understand 'resemblance-based definition'
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term 'ta'rīf shabahī' (resemblance-based definition). This indicates that definitions give a likeness or a distinguishing description, not the real nature. Reflect on how this limits the epistemological value of definitions.
This is a key technical term in his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيُقَال : هذا الذي ذكروه في حدود هذه الأمور هو موجود في سائر ما يرومون بيانه بحدودهم عند التدبر والتأمل. لكنّ منها ما هو بين لكل أحد، كالأمور العامة ؛ ومنها ما هو بين لبعض الناس، كما يعرف أهل الصناعات والمقالات أموراً لا يعرفها غيرهم. فحدودها بالنسبة إلى هؤلاء كحدود تلك الأمور التي يعم علمها بالنسبة إلى العموم .
وأما الأمور التي تخفَى فمجرد الحدود لا تفيد تعريف حقيقتها بعينها، وإنما تفيد تمييزها ونوعاً من التعريف الشَّبَهي .
وهذا يتبين بتقرير قاعدة في ذلك. فنقول :
التحقيق السديد في مسألة التحديد
المقول في جواب ما هو ؟ » المطلوب تعريفه بالحد هو جواب لقول سائل قال : ما كذا!»، كما يقول: «ما الخمر؟»، أو «ما الإنسان؟»، أو «ما الثلج؟» فهذا الا. اسم المستفهم عنه المذكورُ في السؤال إما أن يكون السائل غير عالم بمسمّاه، وإما أن يكون عالماً بمسماه .
مطلوب السائل المتصوّر للمعنى الجاهل بدلالة اللفظ عليه فالأول كالسائل عن اسم في غير لغته أو عن اسم غريب في لغته، أو عن اسم معروف في لغته لكنّ مقصوده تحديد مسماه. مثل العربي إذا سأل عن معاني الأسماء الأعجمية، والعَجَمي إذا سأل عن معاني الأسماء العربية، وبعض الأعاجم إذا سأل بعضاً عن معاني الأسماء التي تكون في لغة المسئول دون السائل. وهذا هو «الترجمة». «الترجمة» وأحكامها : فالمترجم لا بد أن يعرف اللغتين : التي يترجمها، والتي يترجم بها. وإذا عرف أن المعنى الذي يقصد بهذا الاسم في هذه اللغة هو المعنى الذي يقصد به في اللغة الأخرى ترجمه، كما يترجم اسم الخبز والماء، والأكل والشرب، والسماء، والأرض، والليل والنهار والشمس والقمر ونحو ذلك من أسماء الأعيان والأجناس
So it is said: What they have mentioned regarding the definitions of these matters exists in all other matters they aim to explain through their definitions upon reflection and deliberation. But some of them are clear to everyone, like general matters, and some are clear to some people, as the experts of crafts and sciences know things that others do not. So their definitions relative to these people are like the definitions of those matters whose knowledge is common relative to the general public.
As for matters that are hidden, mere definitions do not provide knowledge of their true essence, but only serve to distinguish them and provide a kind of resemblance-based definition.
This is clarified by establishing a principle regarding that. So we say:
The sound investigation concerning the issue of definition: What is said in answer to 'what is it?' — the thing sought to be defined by a definition is an answer to a questioner who says: 'What is such-and-such?' just as one says: 'What is wine?' or 'What is man?' or 'What is snow?' So this is... The name of the thing asked about mentioned in the question is either such that the questioner does not know its referent, or such that he knows its referent.
The first case is that the questioner conceives the meaning but is ignorant of the verbal indication; like one who asks about a name in a language other than his own, or about a foreign name in his language, or about a well-known name in his language but his aim is to define its referent. For example, an Arab asking about the meanings of Persian names, or a Persian asking about the meanings of Arabic names, or some Persians asking others about the meanings of names that are in the language of the respondent but not the questioner. This is 'translation.' 'Translation' and its rulings: The translator must know the two languages: the one from which he translates and the one into which he translates. And if he knows that the meaning intended by this name in this language is the same as the meaning intended in the other language, he translates it, just as he translates the names of bread and water, eating and drinking, sky and earth, night and day, sun and moon, and the like of names of entities and genera.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion where Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between true definition (ḥadd) and translation (tarjama). He begins by stating that definitions are not always revelatory; for hidden matters, they only distinguish, not give the essence. Then he sets a principle: the response to 'what is it?' either answers a question about the name (translation) or about the essence. He gives examples of translation, such as an Arab asking about a Persian word, and notes that translation requires knowing both languages and equivalence of meaning, using common examples like 'bread' and 'water.' This reinforces his critique that much of what logicians call definition is actually mere translation or reminder.

Key Terms
التعريف الشَّبَهي
الحد
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Complete knowledge of the essence.
B) Only distinction and a resemblance-based definition.
C) A direct translation into another language.
D) The true nature of the thing.

Key Takeaway
For hidden matters, definitions are insufficient for essential knowledge; they only distinguish and provide a resemblance.

Consider the Islamic ruling on 'gharar' (uncertainty) in contracts. How does Ibn Taymiyya's view on definitions affect the way scholars define gharar? Does a definition of gharar truly capture its essence or merely distinguish it from explicit terms? Discuss.

Definition versus Translation in Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes definition from translation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a definition (ḥadd) and a translation (tarjama) according to Ibn Taymiyya, using his examples.

Core Idea
Translation explains words, definition explains essences — but they are often confused.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that when someone asks 'What is X?', the answer can either be a translation (if the questioner knows the concept but not the word) or a true definition (if the questioner seeks the essence). He illustrates this with examples of inter-lingual questions. This distinction is crucial for his critique of logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of questioners
Note the distinction: one who does not know the meaning of the word (seeking translation) and one who knows the word but seeks the essence (seeking definition).
2
Examine the examples of translation
Consider an Arab asking about Persian words, or a Persian about Arabic words. These are cases where the concept is known but the term is not. This is translation.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that translation requires knowing both languages and ensuring equivalence.
3
Understand the requirements for translation
The translator must know both languages and verify that the meaning of the word in one language matches the meaning in the other.
He gives examples like bread, water, sun, moon — simple translations.
4
Contrast with true definition
A true definition goes beyond mere word equivalence; it aims to capture the nature (haqiqa) of the thing, not just provide a synonymous expression.
This distinction undercuts many logical definitions as mere translations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definitions Do Not Reveal Essences: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues definitions only distinguish, not define essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's key objection to logicians' definitions and explain why he considers them merely vague distinctions.

Core Idea
Definitions only provide a vague distinction, not the true essence of things.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: that definitions (hudud) reveal the essence of things. He argues that upon reflection, definitions only serve to distinguish things vaguely, especially for hidden matters. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify what the logicians assert about definitions: that they reveal the true essence (haqiqa) of a thing.
This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's observation
He notes that what logicians mention in their definitions is present in all things they explain, but some things are clear to all, some only to specialists.
He distinguishes between common knowledge and specialized knowledge.
3
Distinguish clear vs. hidden matters
Clear matters are evident to everyone; hidden matters are obscure and known only to specialists.
This distinction is key to his argument.
4
Extract what definitions actually provide
For hidden matters, definitions do not give knowledge of the essence, only a distinction (tamyiz) and a vague definition (ta'rif shabahi).
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states: 'mere definitions do not benefit defining their true essence, but they benefit distinguishing them and a type of vague definition.'
5
Note the promised principle
Ibn Taymiyya announces that this point will be clarified by establishing a fundamental principle (taqrir qa'ida).
This sets up further argumentation in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال : هذا الذي ذكروه في حدود هذه الأمور هو موجود في سائر ما يرومون بيانه بحدودهم عند التدبر والتأمل. لكنّ منها ما هو بين لكل أحد، كالأمور العامة ؛ ومنها ما هو بين لبعض الناس، كما يعرف أهل الصناعات والمقالات أموراً لا يعرفها غيرهم. فحدودها بالنسبة إلى هؤلاء كحدود تلك الأمور التي يعم علمها بالنسبة إلى العموم . وأما الأمور التي تخفَى فمجرد الحدود لا تفيد تعريف حقيقتها بعينها، وإنما تفيد تمييزها ونوعاً من التعريف الشَّبَهي . وهذا يتبين بتقرير قاعدة في ذلك.
It is said: This which they mentioned in the definitions of these matters is present in all that they intend to explain by their definitions upon reflection and contemplation. But some of it is clear to everyone, like general matters; and some of it is clear to some people, just as the people of crafts and disciplines know things that others do not know. Their definitions relative to these are like the definitions of those matters whose knowledge is common relative to the general public. As for matters that are hidden, mere definitions do not benefit defining their true essence, but they benefit distinguishing them and a type of vague definition. This will be made clear by establishing a principle regarding that.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide knowledge of essences. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that what the logicians mention in their definitions is present in all things they aim to explain upon scrutiny. However, some things are evident to everyone, like universal matters, while others are known only to specialists, such as artisans or scholars. Their definitions for specialists are akin to common definitions for the public—they are useful but not revelatory. Regarding hidden matters, definitions alone do not yield knowledge of the true essence (haqiqa); they only give a distinction (tamyiz) and a vague, approximate definition (ta'rif shabahi). He then announces that this will be clarified by establishing a fundamental principle (taqrir qa'ida). The argument is that definitions are not the ultimate source of certain knowledge; they serve a limited role.

Key Terms

تعريف شبَهي

Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions serve only to distinguish things vaguely, not to reveal their true essences.

Ibn Taymiyya's Classification of Definitional Questions
Ibn Taymiyya classifies definitional questions and introduces translation as a type of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitional questions into two types according to Ibn Taymiyya and identify when a definition is actually a translation.

Core Idea
Many 'definitions' are just translations – Ibn Taymiyya expands definition to include linguistic clarification.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his investigation into definition by analyzing the very question 'What is it?' He shows that not all such questions seek the essence; some merely ask for the meaning of a word. This passage lays the groundwork for his broader critique of Greek logic's obsession with essential definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question type
Recognize that the definitional question 'What is it?' can have two distinct contexts: the questioner may be ignorant of the referent of the name, or may already know the referent but seek a more precise definition.
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: 'What is wine?', 'What is man?', 'What is snow?'
2
Distinguish between the two states of the questioner
The first case is when the questioner does not know what the name refers to (musamma). The second is when the questioner knows the referent but seeks a definition (hadd).
The first case includes asking about a name in a foreign language or a strange name in one's own language.
3
Focus on the first type as translation
Ibn Taymiyya identifies the first type as 'translation' (tarjama). It is not an essential definition but a linguistic clarification of the word's meaning.
For example, an Arab asking about a non-Arabic word, or a non-Arab asking about an Arabic word.
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Given a real scenario, determine whether the question 'What is X?' is requesting a translation or an essential definition. Use Ibn Taymiyya's criterion: does the questioner know the referent?
Practice with a fiqh term like 'riba'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : التحقيق السديد في مسألة التحديد المقول في جواب ما هو ؟ » المطلوب تعريفه بالحد هو جواب لقول سائل قال : ما كذا!»، كما يقول: «ما الخمر؟»، أو «ما الإنسان؟»، أو «ما الثلج؟» فهذا الا. اسم المستفهم عنه المذكورُ في السؤال إما أن يكون السائل غير عالم بمسمّاه، وإما أن يكون عالماً بمسماه . مطلوب السائل المتصوّر للمعنى الجاهل بدلالة اللفظ عليه فالأول كالسائل عن اسم في غير لغته أو عن اسم غريب في لغته، أو عن اسم معروف في لغته لكنّ مقصوده تحديد مسماه. مثل العربي إذا سأل عن معاني الأسماء الأعجمية، والعَجَمي إذا سأل عن معاني الأسماء العربية، وبعض الأعاجم إذا سأل بعضاً عن معاني الأسماء التي تكون في لغة المسئول دون السائل. وهذا هو «الترجمة».
We say: The correct investigation regarding the matter of definition: what is said in answer to 'What is it?' The thing sought to be defined by a definition is an answer to the statement of a questioner who says: 'What is such-and-such?' as he says: 'What is wine?' or 'What is man?' or 'What is snow?' So this. The name of the thing asked about mentioned in the question is either such that the questioner does not know its referent, or he knows its referent. The request of the questioner who conceptualizes the meaning but is ignorant of the indication of the word for it—the first is like the questioner about a name in a language other than his, or about a strange name in his language, or about a known name in his language but his intent is to determine its referent. For example, an Arab asking about the meanings of non-Arabic names, and a non-Arab asking about meanings of Arabic names, and some non-Arabs asking others about meanings of names that are in the language of the one asked and not the asker. This is 'translation'.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the promised principle (taqrir qa'ida) for understanding definitions. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that the correct investigation into definition (al-tahqiq al-sadid fi mas'alat al-tahdid) concerns what is said in response to the question 'What is it?' (ma huwa?). He gives examples: 'What is wine?', 'What is man?', 'What is snow?' The name (ism) of the thing asked about can be either unknown to the questioner in terms of its referent (musamma) or known. The request of the questioner who already has a conceptualization (tasawwur) of the meaning but is ignorant of the linguistic indication of the word falls into the first category. This includes: asking about a name in a foreign language, a strange word in one's own language, or a familiar word used to determine its specific referent. He gives examples of an Arab asking about meanings of non-Arabic words, or a non-Arab asking about Arabic words, or one non-Arab asking another about a word in the respondent's language. He identifies this as 'translation' (al-tarjama). Thus, for Ibn Taymiyya, many definitional requests are actually requests for translation, not for essential definitions.

Key Terms
تحديد
ترجمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Asking about the essence of a thing
B) Asking about the linguistic meaning of a name
C) Asking about the genus and differentia
D) Asking about the purpose of a thing

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines 'definition' to include translation, showing that many apparent definitional questions are merely requests for linguistic equivalence.

Translation as a Verbal Definition: Ibn Taymiyya's Practical Epistemology
Ibn Taymiyya on translation as practical definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential definitions (hadd haqiqi) and verbal definitions (hadd lafzi), and explain why Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the latter for understanding scholarly texts.

Core Idea
Translation is not exact; it is a useful approximation, and that is enough for understanding the sciences.

Ibn Taymiyya shifts the focus from perfect essential definitions to practical verbal definitions like tarjama. In this passage, he explains the translator's task and the limitations of translation, especially for Arabic. He ties this to the broader project of understanding texts in the Islamic sciences. This lesson will help you see why verbal definitions are indispensable for scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the translator's conditions
Ibn Taymiyya states the translator must know both the source and target languages. He also needs to know whether the meaning intended by a term in one language matches the meaning in the other.
This condition is about semantic equivalence, not just lexical.
2
Examine the examples of translatable terms
He gives examples like bread, water, sky, earth – terms for concrete entities and sensible concepts. These are relatively easy to translate because the referents are common across cultures.
Notice these are physical, observable things.
3
Understand the scope of translation
Translation applies to both single words (mufradat) and complete, composed speech (kalam mu'allaf tamm). This shows that even complex sentences can be translated, though with difficulty.
The distinction between words and speech is important for tafsir.
4
Recognize the approximate nature of translation
Ibn Taymiyya says translation is often approximate (taqrib) because exact equivalents may not exist, especially for Arabic. This is a key limitation to acknowledge.
Approximation does not mean useless; it is still informative.
5
Connect translation to tafsir and gharib al-Quran
The study of obscure words (gharib) in Quran and hadith, and even tafsir, falls under this category of verbal definition. The goal is to understand the speaker's intent.
This shows that translation is a core tool for all Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الترجمة وأحكامها المترجم لا بد أن يعرف اللغتين : التي يترجمها، والتي يترجم بها. وإذا عرف أن المعنى الذي يقصد بهذا الاسم في هذه اللغة هو المعنى الذي يقصد به في اللغة الأخرى ترجمه، كما يترجم اسم الخبز والماء، والأكل والشرب، والسماء، والأرض، والليل والنهار والشمس والقمر ونحو ذلك من أسماء الأعيان والأجناس وما تضمنته من الأشخاص، سواء كانت مسمياتها أعياناً أو معاني . والترجمة تكون لـ «المفردات» ولـ «الكلام المؤلف التام» . وإن كان كثير من الترجمة لا يأتي بحقيقة المعنى التي في تلك اللغة، بل بالتّرجمة تقريب في الغالب يقاربه، لأن تلك المعاني قد لا تكون لها في اللغة الأخرى ألفاظ تطابقها على الحقيقة ؛ لا سيما لغة العرب فإن ترجمتها في الغالب تقريب. هذا الباب ذكر غريب القرآن والحديث وغيرهما. بل وتفسير القرآن، غريب القرآن ومن وغيره من سائر أنواع الكلام، وهو في أول درجاته من هذا الباب، فإن المقصود ذكر مراد المتكلّم بتلك الأسماء، أو بذلك الكلام. وتفسيره من هذا الباب. وهذا الحد، هم متفقون على أنه من الحدود اللفظية مع أن هذا هو الذي الترجمة من أنواع يُحتاج إليه في إقراء العلوم المصنفة، بل في قراءة جميع الكتب، بل في جميع المخاطبات فإن من قرأ كتب النحو والطب، أو غيرهما، لا بد أن يعرف مراد أصحابها بتلك الأسماء، ويعرف مرادهم بالكلام المؤلف وكذلك من قرأ كتب الفقه والكلام، والفلسفة. وغير ذلك.
Translation and its rulings: The translator must know the two languages: the one he translates from and the one he translates into. And if he knows that the meaning intended by this name in this language is the same meaning intended in the other language, he translates it, just as he translates the name of bread and water, eating and drinking, sky and earth, night and day, sun and moon, and similar names of entities and genera and what they contain of individuals, whether their referents are entities or meanings. Translation is for 'simple terms' and for 'complete composed speech'. Although much translation does not bring the exact reality of the meaning in that language, but rather translation is mostly approximate, because those meanings may not have words in the other language that truly correspond to them, especially the language of the Arabs, for their translation is mostly approximate. This section includes the discussion of obscure words of the Quran and hadith and others. Indeed, even the interpretation (tafsir) of the Quran, the obscure words of the Quran and others from all types of speech, is at the first stages of this section, for the intent is to mention the speaker's intention by those names or by that speech. And its interpretation is from this section. And this definition, they agree that it is among the verbal definitions, even though this is what translation is among the types needed in teaching the classified sciences, indeed in reading all books, indeed in all communications. For whoever reads books of grammar and medicine, or others, must know the intention of their authors by those names, and know their intention by the composed speech. Similarly, whoever reads books of fiqh and kalam and philosophy, and so on.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage elaborates on translation (tarjama) as a type of definition. Ibn Taymiyya states the translator must know both languages: the source and target. When he knows that the meaning intended by a name in one language matches the meaning in the other, he can translate. Examples are common concrete nouns: bread, water, sky, etc. Translation applies to both single words (mufradat) and complete sentences (kalam mu'allaf tamm). However, translation is often approximate (taqrib) because exact equivalents may not exist, especially in Arabic. This section includes the study of obscure words (gharib) in Quran and hadith, and even tafsir (interpretation) falls under this category. The goal is to understand the speaker's intent. This type of definition is agreed to be a 'verbal definition' (hadd lafzi). It is essential for teaching all sciences and for reading any book. Whether grammar, medicine, fiqh, kalam, or philosophy, one must understand the author's intended meanings for terms and phrases. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the practical necessity of this kind of definition in all scholarly pursuits.

حد لفظي

Key Takeaway
Translation is an approximate verbal definition that is indispensable for understanding texts in all Islamic sciences.

Legal Definitions as a Religious Obligation
Ibn Taymiyya asserts the religious duty of knowing legal definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fard 'ayn and fard kifaya in the context of knowing legal definitions from the Quran and Sunnah, and identify the religious basis for this distinction as presented by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Knowing definitions of Quranic terms is a religious duty, not a luxury.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the notion that definitions are merely logical tools. He anchors the study of definitions in the Quran and Sunnah, making it a religious obligation. In this passage, he distinguishes between individual and communal duties regarding this knowledge, and provides a Quranic proof and examples of strange words that require explanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's central claim
Read the first sentence: 'Knowledge of the legal definitions is from the religion.' This establishes that understanding definitions is not optional but a religious duty.
2
Distinguish the two types of obligation
Note that Ibn Taymiyya says knowledge may be fard 'ayn (individual) or fard kifaya (communal). Think about what kind of knowledge every Muslim must know versus what suffices if some scholars know.
Fard 'ayn applies to essential terms like 'salat' and 'zakat'; fard kifaya applies to rare terms like 'ḍīzā'.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
Find the verse quoted (Quran 9:97) where Allah blames those who do not know the 'limits' (hudud) of what He revealed. This verse is used as evidence that ignorance of definitions is blameworthy.
The verse condemns Bedouins for not knowing these limits.
4
Recognize the implication for gharib al-Quran
Ibn Taymiyya mentions strange words like 'ḍīzā', 'qaswarah', and 'ʿasʿasa'. This shows that part of knowing legal definitions includes studying gharib (unusual) vocabulary, which is a discipline in tafsir.
These words require scholarly explanation, hence fard kifaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معرفة الحدود الشرعية من الدين وهذه الحدود معرفتُها من الدين، في كل لفظ هو في كتاب الله تعالى وسنة رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلم . ثم قد تكون معرفتها فرض عين، وقد تكون فرضَ كفايه . ولهذا ذم الله تعالى من لم يعرف هذه الحدود بقوله تعالى : ﴿ الْأَعْرَابُ أَشَدُّ كُفْرًا وَنِفَاقًا وَأَجْدَرُ ألَّا يَعْلَمُوا حُدُودَ مَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ عَلَى رَسُولِهِ) [سورة التوبة : ٩٧]. والذي أنزله على رسوله فيه ما قد يكون الاسم غريباً بالنسبة إلى المستمع، الأسماء الغريبة كلفظ ضِيزَى و قَسْوَرَة و عَسْعَسَ وأمثال ذلك .
Knowledge of the legal definitions (al-hudud al-shar'iyya) is from the religion. Knowledge of these definitions is from the religion, regarding every word that is in the Book of Allah Most High and the Sunnah of His Messenger, peace be upon him. Then its knowledge may be an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) or a communal obligation (fard kifaya). For this reason, Allah Most High blamed those who do not know these definitions in His saying: 'The Bedouins are more severe in disbelief and hypocrisy and more likely not to know the limits of what Allah has revealed to His Messenger' [Surah al-Tawba 9:97]. And what He revealed to His Messenger includes what may be a strange word to the listener, strange words like 'ḍīzā', 'qaswarah', 'ʿasʿasa', and the like.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage turns to the religious significance of definitions. Ibn Taymiyya states that knowledge of legal definitions (al-hudud al-shar'iyya) is part of the religion (min al-din). This applies to every word in the Quran and Sunnah. Knowing these definitions may be an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) or a communal obligation (fard kifaya) depending on the context. He supports this with a Quranic verse (9:97) that blames those who do not know the limits (hudud) of what Allah revealed. Then he notes that the revealed text includes strange words (gharib) such as 'ḍīzā' (a word from Surah al-Najm), 'qaswarah' (Surah al-Muddaththir), and 'ʿasʿasa' (Surah al-Takwir). These require explanation. Thus, for Ibn Taymiyya, understanding definitions is not just a logical exercise but a religious duty. The study of gharib al-Quran and tafsir is a necessary part of knowing these definitions.

Key Terms
حد شرعي
فرض عين

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya establishes that knowledge of legal definitions from revelation is an essential religious duty, varying between individual and communal obligation.

Why Translation Fails for Shar'i Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya critiques translation and establishes knowing Shar'i definitions as a religious duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of translation as a means to define Shar'i terms and explain why revelation is necessary for precise boundaries.

Core Idea
Translation is approximation; only revelation gives the exact boundaries of Shar'i terms.

Ibn Taymiyya begins the fourth aspect by critiquing translation, an implicit attack on logicians who relied on translated texts. He argues that translation cannot capture the true meaning (haqiqa) of Arabic Shar'i terms; it only approximates. This is not merely a linguistic observation but a religious one: knowing the precise boundaries (hudud) of Allah's commands is a religious duty, because these terms come from revelation, not from translation or logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about translation
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that translation is only taqrib (approximation). He says: 'إن كثير من الترجمة لا يأتي بحقيقة المعنى التي في تلك اللغة'.
2
Extract his reasoning for why translation fails
He gives two reasons: (a) those meanings may not have corresponding words in the target language, and (b) Arabic is especially untranslatable. Note that this undermines the logicians' project of using translated Greek definitions for Islamic concepts.
3
Connect the critique to religious obligation
Notice how he immediately pivots to 'معرفة الحدود الشرعية من الدين' – the knowledge of these boundaries is part of religion. This connects his linguistic critique to a theological imperative: you cannot rely on translation; you must go to the sources.
4
Identify the Quranic proof (9:97)
He cites the verse criticizing the bedouins for not knowing the hudud. This shows that ignorance of precise definitions is blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : معرفة الحدود الشرعية من الدين
وإن كان كثير من الترجمة لا يأتي بحقيقة المعنى التي في تلك اللغة، بل بما الترجمة تقريب في الغالب يقاربه، لأن تلك المعاني قد لا تكون لها في اللغة الأخرى ألفاظ تطابقها على الحقيقة ؛ لا سيما لغة العرب فإن ترجمتها في الغالب تقريب.
وهذا الحد، هم متفقون على أنه من الحدود اللفظية مع أن هذا هو الذي يحتاج إليه في إقراء العلوم المصنفة، بل في قراءة جميع الكتب، بل في جميع المخاطبات فإن من قرأ كتب النحو والطب، أو غيرهما، لا بد أن يعرف مراد أصحابها بتلك الأسماء، ويعرف مرادهم بالكلام المؤلف وكذلك من قرأ كتب الفقه والكلام، والفلسفة. وغير ذلك.
معرفة الحدود الشرعية من الدين
وهذه الحدود معرفتُها من الدين، في كل لفظ هو في كتاب الله تعالى وسنة رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلم . ثم قد تكون معرفتها فرض عين، وقد تكون فرضَ كفايه . ولهذا ذم الله تعالى من لم يعرف هذه الحدود بقوله تعالى : ﴿ الْأَعْرَابُ أَشَدُّ كُفْرًا وَنِفَاقًا وَأَجْدَرُ ألَّا يَعْلَمُوا حُدُودَ مَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ عَلَى رَسُولِهِ) [سورة التوبة : ۹۷].
والذي أنزله على رسوله فيه ما قد يكون الاسم غريباً بالنسبة إلى المستمع، الأسماء الغريبة كلفظ «ضِيزَى» و «قَسْوَرَة» و «عَسْعَسَ، وأمثال ذلك .
وقد يكون مشهوراً ، لكن لا يعلم حده ، بل يعلم معناه على سبيل الإجمال، كاسم «الصلوة» و«الزكوة» و«الصيام» والحج. فإن هذه، وإن كان جمهور المخاطبين يعلمون معناها على سبيل الإجمال، فلا يعلمون مسمّاها على سبيل التحديد الجامع المانع إلا من جهة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وهي التي يقال لها: «الأسماء الشرعية». كما إذا قيل : صلوة «الجنازة وسجدتا السهو» و «سجود الشكر» و«الطواف هل تدخل في مسمى «الصلوة» في قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مفتاح الصلوة الطهور، وتحريمها التكبير، وتحليلها التسليم؟ فقيل : كل ذلك صلوة» تجب لها «الطهارة»، وقيل لا تجب الطهارة لشيء من ذلك؟ وقيل تجب لِما تحريمه التكبير وتحليله التسليم ، كـ«صلوة الجنازة وسجدتي السهو» ، دون الطوافِ» و «سجود التلاوة» ! وكذلك اسم «الخمر» و«الربوا» و«المَيْسِر»، ونحو ذلك. تعلم أشياء من مسمياتها، ومنها ما لا يعلم إلا ببيان آخر. فإنه قد يكون الشيء داخلاً في اسم «الربوا» والمَيْسِر والإنسان لا يعلم ذلك إلا بدليل على ذلك ـ شرعي، أو غيره .
حد «الغيبة» ومن هذا قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، لما سُئل عن حد «الغيبة»، فقال: «ذِكْرُكَ أخاك بما يكره. فقال له أرأيت إن كان في أخي ما أقول؟» فقال: «إن كان فيه ما تقول فقد اغْتَبْتَه ، وإن لم يكن فيه ما تقول فقد بَهَنَّه»
وكذلك قوله لما قال : «لا يدخل الجنة مَنْ في قلبه مثقال ذرة من كبر». فسأله الصحابي عن حده.
The second station - the fourth aspect: knowledge of Shar'i definitions from religion. And although much translation does not bring the true meaning that is in that language, rather the translation is an approximation in most cases because those meanings may not have in the other language words that exactly correspond to them; especially the language of the Arabs, for its translation is in most cases approximation. And this definition, they agree that it is among the verbal definitions, and this is what is needed in teaching the classified sciences, rather in reading all books, rather in all addresses, for whoever reads books of grammar and medicine, etc., must know the intended meaning of their authors with those names, and know their intended meaning with the composite speech. Similarly, whoever reads books of fiqh, kalam, philosophy, and other such. Knowledge of Shar'i definitions from religion. And these definitions, knowing them is from religion, for every word that is in the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger. Then their knowledge may be an individual obligation, and may be a collective obligation. For this reason Allah criticized those who do not know these definitions by His saying: 'The bedouins are the most severe in disbelief and hypocrisy, and more likely not to know the boundaries of what Allah has revealed to His Messenger.' [Surah at-Tawbah: 97]. And what He revealed to His Messenger includes what may be a strange name for the listener: the strange names like the word 'ḍīzā' and 'qaswara' and 'ʿasʿasa', and the like. And there may be a well-known term, but its precise limit is not known, rather its meaning is known in summary, like the name 'prayer', 'zakah', 'fasting', and 'pilgrimage'. For these, although the majority of addressees know their meaning in summary, they do not know their precise referent in a comprehensive and exclusive way except from the Messenger. And these are what are called 'Shar'i names'. As when it is said: the funeral prayer, the two prostrations of forgetfulness, the prostration of thanksgiving, and circumambulation — are they included in the name 'prayer' in his saying: 'The key to prayer is purification, its prohibition is the takbir, and its permission is the taslim'? Then it was said: all of that is prayer, for which purification is obligatory; and it was said: purification is not obligatory for any of that; and it was said: it is obligatory for that whose prohibition is takbir and permission is taslim, like the funeral prayer and the two prostrations of forgetfulness, but not circumambulation and the prostration of recitation. Similarly the name 'wine', 'riba', 'gambling', and the like. Some of their referents are known, and some are not known except by another statement. For a thing may be included in the name 'riba' or 'gambling', and a person does not know that except by a proof — Shar'i or other. The definition of 'backbiting': and from this is the saying of the Prophet, when he was asked about the definition of backbiting, he said: 'Your mention of your brother with what he dislikes.' The questioner said: 'Do you think if what I say is indeed in my brother?' He said: 'If what you say is in him, then you have backbitten him; and if what you say is not in him, then you have slandered him.' Similarly his saying when he said: 'No one enters the Paradise who has in his heart a grain's weight of pride.' Then the companion asked him about its definition.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of definitions, specifically Shar'i definitions, by Ibn Taymiyya. He begins by addressing the fourth aspect of the second station: knowledge of Shar'i definitions is part of religion. He immediately critiques the notion of translation, stating that most translations are approximations and do not capture the exact meaning of the original language, especially Arabic. This is relevant because logicians often relied on translation to define terms, but Ibn Taymiyya argues that for religious texts, precise knowledge of boundaries (hudud) is essential and cannot be achieved through mere linguistic translation. He then asserts that knowing these definitions is a religious duty, varying between individual and collective obligation, and he cites Quran 9:97 to criticize those ignorant of Allah's boundaries. The passage then distinguishes between strange words (gharib) that are unfamiliar and well-known words like 'salat' and 'zakat' which are known in summary but whose precise definitions (al-jami' al-mani') come only from the Prophet. This introduces the concept of 'al-asma' al-shar'iyya'. Ibn Taymiyya illustrates with examples: whether 'funeral prayer', 'prostration of forgetfulness', etc., fall under 'salat' as defined by the Prophet's hadith. Different opinions exist. Similarly, terms like 'khamr', 'riba', 'maysir' require further clarification. Finally, he gives the Prophet's definition of 'ghiba' (backbiting) and mentions the definition of 'kibr' (pride) to show that even commonly known moral concepts require precise Shar'i definitions. The overall point is that logical definitions alone are insufficient; Shar'i definitions are revealed and must be known from the sources.

Key Terms
حد
الحدود الشرعية
الأسماء الشرعية
الترجمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is translation of Arabic terms mostly an approximation?
A) Because translators lack skill
B) Because meanings may have no exact equivalents in other languages
C) Because language changes over time
D) Because Arabic has too many words

Key Takeaway
Translation cannot give the exact meaning of Shar'i terms; only revelation can provide their precise boundaries (hudud).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read Ibn Taymiyya's critique of translation and then examine the difference between the linguistic and Shar'i definitions of 'salat' in a work of Hanbali fiqh. Identify which parts of the definition come from revelation.

Prophetic Definitions: The Model for Shar'i Terms
Ibn Taymiyya illustrates revealed definitions with Prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the structure of a Prophetic definition (like that of ghiba) and apply it to other Shar'i terms.

Core Idea
The Prophet's definitions give the complete boundary (hadd) of Shar'i terms, leaving no ambiguity for rulings.

After establishing that translation fails and definitions come from revelation, Ibn Taymiyya provides several examples of how the Prophet defined key Shar'i terms. The most detailed is the definition of 'ghiba' (backbiting): 'Your mention of your brother with what he dislikes.' This definition is precise: it includes the genus (ذِكْرُكَ) and the differentia (بما يكره), and it handles the case when the statement is true or false. This shows that a proper Shar'i definition is comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani').

Guided Path
1
Locate the Prophetic definition of ghiba
In the passage, find the hadith where the Prophet says: 'ذكرك أخاك بما يكره'.
2
Identify the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl)
The genus is 'mentioning' (dhikr), the differentia is 'what your brother dislikes'. Together they form the complete definition.
3
Analyze the Prophet's response to the follow-up question
The questioner asks if the statement is true, the Prophet says it's still backbiting; if false, it's slander. This clarifies the boundary.
4
Compare with other examples (salat, riba)
Notice that for these, Ibn Taymiyya says their precise referents are known only from the Prophet, and he mentions disputes over whether certain acts (like funeral prayer) fall under 'salat'.
